A log shifted in the stove, sending up a shower of sparks. MacAulay felt the weight of what was being offered. It was not a weapon. It was a blueprint. A schematic of the edifice of power, with certain load-bearing walls highlighted in red.

“This is treason,” MacAulay said, the word hanging in the smoky air.

“It is a realignment of priority,” Ross replied. “Is it treason for a doctor to ignore a hospital’s bureaucracy to save a patient? The British state is not Britain. It is a system that governs it. Sometimes, to save the patient, you must bypass the system. Temporarily. Strategically.”

“To what end? You collapse a few systems, cause some chaos. Then what? You don’t have an army. You don’t have a political mandate.”

“We have you,” Fraser said simply.

The silence that followed was absolute. The wind outside had dropped. The only sound was the faint hiss of the gas.

“You are the legitimacy,” Ross continued, his eyes fixed on MacAulay. “The elected First Minister. In the event of a catastrophic, systemic failure of the apparatus of government—a failure not of your making—you would have the constitutional responsibility, and the moral authority, to assume emergency powers to ensure stability, continuity, and the safety of the people. To restore order on new terms. The terms of the actual social contract, not the administrative one.”

MacAulay laughed, a short, sharp bark that held no humour. “You are insane. You are describing a coup.”

“We are describing a *catalyst*,” Ross insisted, leaning forward. The lamplight deepened the hollows of his face. “The British state is undergoing a slow-motion collapse of legitimacy. It is hollowed out by corruption, by nostalgia, by a profound disconnect between its governing class and the governed. It will fall. The only question is what follows. Will it be a chaotic, violent rupture, exploited by the worst elements? Or can it be a managed, deliberate transition? A return of sovereignty not to a flag, but to the ground under our feet? We are offering you the tools to guide that transition. To be the author of what comes next, not its victim.”

MacAulay stood up. The stool scraped on the stone floor. He walked to the small, grimy window, looked out at the black lochan, the star-strung sky. The peace of the accounted-for was gone, shattered. In its place was this: a conspiracy in a bothy, offering him the keys to a kingdom of chaos. The allure of agency was no longer a flame. It was a wildfire, and they were asking him to hold the torch.

“Why me?” he asked the glass.

“Because you are a pragmatist, not an ideologue,” Ross said from behind him. “Because you understand power, and you are already sickened by its current flavour. Because you stood at that wall, Alistair. You looked at the map. You took the papers. You came here tonight. You are already walking on the frost. We are merely suggesting a direction.”

MacAulay turned. “And if I say no? If I walk out of here and call the Security Service?”

Calum looked up from his map for the first time. His eyes were a flat, calm grey. “Then you walk out. The card is paper. This conversation never happened. We are ghosts. But the frost will still be there. And one night, you will be standing on it when it cracks. And you will fall, with the rest of them, into the dark. We are offering you a rope.”

“Manufactured by you.”

“The only rope there is,” Fraser said.

MacAulay looked at each of them: the professor, the soldier, the keeper, the silent woman by the door. They were not fanatics. Their eyes were clear, calculating. They were engineers of consequence. He thought of the Prime Minister’s ‘collegiate’ tone. The smooth, smiling knife. He thought of the room of locked doors. He thought of the weight of the stone in his hand. *Throw it, or don’t.*

He did not have a stone. He had a card with a grid reference.

“I am not saying yes,” MacAulay said, his voice low. “I am saying I will listen.”

Ross nodded, as if this was the only answer he had expected. “Then listen to this. The devolution review. The ‘collegiate’ bilateral. It is a facade. The Prime Minister’s strategy is no longer to block or bully. It is to absorb. To integrate the Scottish government’s functions so seamlessly into a new ‘UK-wide framework’ for energy, trade, and security that devolution becomes a technical, not a political, reality. A spoke in the wheel, not a separate engine. They will offer you more money, more ‘co-operation’. In return, you will quietly surrender the very levers that make your government meaningful. It will be death by embrace. Within eighteen months, your office will be a glorified county council. A tourist attraction.”

“How do you know this?”

“Because I wrote the first draft of the strategy paper,” Ross said. “Five years ago. It was considered too subtle then. Now, with the current political fractures, it is seen as elegant. Inevitable. The final document is on the Permanent Secretary’s desk. Sir Jeremy Cathcart has had it for a week.”

The words landed in MacAulay’s gut like a stone. *Cathcart.* The polished neutrality. The well-cut overcoat of a knighthood. He saw the man arranging invisible lint, guiding the river of detail. A man who knew the destination of every stream.

“You’re certain.”

“The document reference is CAB/114/77. It is in a blue folder, not the red one he will show you. He is waiting for the right psychological moment to present it—likely after a good dinner, when you are feeling the warmth of their collegiality. He will frame it as an opportunity. A historic integration.”

MacAulay believed him. The specificity was a bullet. It had the cold, certain heft of truth.

“What is your proposal?” The question left his lips before he could call it back. He was no longer orbiting the point. He was falling towards it.

Calum tapped his pencil on the map. “A demonstration. A small one. To prove our capability, and to show you the nature of the leverage. A targeted, non-violent intervention. It will cause a significant, temporary inconvenience to a key Westminster project. It will be untraceable. And it will carry a signature that only you will understand.”

“What signature?”

“The blank page,” Fraser said. “A void where their power is supposed to be.”

“And if I refuse?”

“Then the demonstration does not happen. We remain ghosts. And you go to your meeting with Cathcart and the Prime Minister, and you accept the blue folder, and you begin the process of presiding over your own political euthanasia.”

MacAulay walked back to the stool. He sat down heavily. The heat from the stove was oppressive now, cloying. He felt the sweat trickle down his spine. This was the moment. The *how far*. He could stand up, walk out into the clean, cold dark, and drive back to the world of folders and agendas and slow, respectable surrender. Or he could take a step onto the glittering, treacherous surface they were mapping for him.

He thought of the raven. Watching. Remembering.

“A demonstration,” he repeated. “Non-violent. Contained.”

“Contained,” Ross confirmed.

“And I give no order. I simply… do not object.”

“Your silence is the permission. Your continued presence here is the acquiescence.”

MacAulay looked at the mug of tea, now cold. He saw his own distorted reflection in the dark surface. He did not recognise the man looking back.

“When?” he asked.

Calum looked at his watch. “In approximately forty-seven hours.”

So soon. It was real. It was moving. He had not thrown the stone. But he had nodded to the man who would throw it for him. The corruption was not in the act. It was in the space between the question and the answer. It was in the quiet of the engaged.

He stood again. “I need to go.”

No one moved to stop him. The woman by the door straightened, ready to lead him back. Fraser gave him a last, slow nod. Ross’s eyes held a strange, paternal pity.

“The frost is beautiful, Alistair,” the old professor said softly. “But it is not the land. Remember which one you serve.”

MacAulay walked out into the night. The cold was a physical blow, washing away the stifling heat of the bothy, but not the fever in his mind. The walk back to the car was a dream. The stars were pitiless. The woman left him at the edge of the parking area, melting back into the shadows without a word.

He drove for an hour before he pulled over on a deserted stretch of road, shaking so violently he had to grip the steering wheel to still himself. He opened the car door and vomited onto the gravel verge, the bitter tea and the acid taste of conspiracy. He spat, wiped his mouth, stared up at the indifferent stars.

He had not joined anything. He had not given an answer.

But he had listened. And in that room, listening was the contract.

When he finally arrived back at the cottage, the sky to the east was showing the first faint smear of grey. He was exhausted, hollowed out. He went to the table. The card was still there. He picked it up. It was just paper. It had no power. He had given it all.

He placed it back on the table. He did not burn it.

He went to the window. The dawn was coming, a slow seep of light into the world. The frost was thick on his own grass, glittering, beautiful, treacherous. He had felt the ground give. Now he had agreed to test its strength.

In his pocket, his encrypted government phone vibrated. He pulled it out. A message from Sir Jeremy Cathcart.

*First Minister. A thought occurred overnight. The PM’s office has hinted at a significant new proposal on shared energy infrastructure. I’ve taken the liberty of drafting some preliminary thoughts. I’ll bring the relevant folder to you at 10 a.m. It’s a blue one. I think you’ll find it most illuminating.*

MacAulay stared at the screen. The words were innocuous. Professional. The blue folder. CAB/114/77. The death by embrace.

He did not reply. He watched the message until the screen went dark, reflecting his own face back at him—a pale, haunted oval in the pre-dawn gloom.

The hook for the next chapter was not a question. It was a countdown. Forty-seven hours. And a blue folder on his desk at ten.



---

# Chapter 15

## **CHAPTER 15**

The blue folder sat on the desk in the private office at St Andrew’s House. It was a cold, institutional blue, the colour of a deep winter loch under a clouded sky. MacAulay did not touch it. He stood at the window, looking down over Edinburgh’s New Town, the Georgian geometry of it laid out like a promise of order. The frost of the morning had not lifted; it clung to the rooftops and the bare branches of the trees in the gardens, a gilded lie that would melt into grey slurry by noon.

He had slept for two hours, a thin and troubled sleep haunted by the image of the woman’s hands—practical, unadorned—placing a cup of bitter tea on a scarred table. The memory had the texture of a stone in his palm. He flexed his hand now, feeling the ghost of that weight.

A soft knock. The door opened before he could answer. Sir Jeremy Cathcart entered, a second, thinner file tucked under his arm. He closed the door with a precise, soundless click.

“First Minister. I trust the journey in was uneventful.”

“The roads were quiet,” MacAulay said, not turning. “Frost makes people careful. Or it sends them into ditches. There’s no middle ground.”

“A pertinent metaphor,” Cathcart said. MacAulay heard the faint rustle of the man settling into a chair, the whisper of wool on leather. “The folder, as promised.”

MacAulay turned. Cathcart was not looking at the blue folder. He was looking at MacAulay, his expression one of polite, focused interest, like a doctor observing symptoms.

“Illuminating, you said.”

“I believe so. It concerns the proposed North Sea Grid Interconnector. A joint UK-Scotland-Norway venture. On the surface, a textbook piece of strategic cooperation. Renewable energy sharing, market stability, security of supply. The Prime Minister is… keen. Exceptionally so.”

MacAulay walked to the desk. He rested his fingertips on the cool cardboard of the folder. “And below the surface?”

Cathcart steepled his fingers. “The financing model is Byzantine. A series of shell companies, layered debt, guarantees. The initial capital is to be raised through a Crown Entity bond, issued discreetly. The ultimate liability, however, flows one way. To the Scottish Government’s balance sheet. It is a masterclass in off-book obligation. If it were to fail—and the initial projections are, in my view, criminally optimistic—the political and financial cost would be… existential. For this administration.”

MacAulay opened the folder. The first page was a summary, dense with figures and legal citations. He did not read them. He looked at the header: *Project Trident*. The name was a blunt instrument.

“Why show me this? This is the ‘embrace’. You warned me of it.”

“I warned you of a pattern. This is the specific mechanism. Knowledge is not complicity, First Minister. It is forewarning.” Cathcart’s voice was a dry, administrative monotone. “The Prime Minister will present it as a gift. A significant new power, a shared venture. He will ask for your enthusiastic, public support. He will imply that hesitation would be a betrayal of the national interest—both Britains, if you will.”

“And if I refuse?”

“Then the proposal will be shelved. With regret. And the narrative will become one of missed opportunity, of parochialism. The devolution review negotiations, which begin in forty-six hours, would proceed under that cloud. Your leverage diminishes.” Cathcart paused. “If you accept, you bind your government to a financial sword of Damocles. One whose cord he holds.”

MacAulay turned a page. A map, showing thick lines snaking across the seabed from Aberdeen to Bergen, with a spider’s web of connections back to England. A hydra. “And you, Jeremy? Where do you stand in this?”

“I am a civil servant. I stand where I am placed. My duty is to ensure you are apprised of the facts as they are, not as one might wish them to be.” He adjusted the cuff of his shirt. “My… allegiance is to the integrity of the administration. This proposal threatens that integrity. Therefore, I bring it to you.”

It was the closest the man had ever come to declaring a side. MacAulay heard the unspoken text, the sub-clause: *for now*.

“What’s in the other file?”

Cathcart glanced at the slim folder on his knee. “A contingency. Should you wish to… test the ground, as it were. A discreet inquiry into the ultimate beneficial ownership of the primary shell company. It is a tortuous path. It leads, I suspect, to a private equity consortium with very close ties to the Prime Minister’s party treasurer. And to several members of the House of Lords who sit on the Defence Select Committee.”

“Blackmail.”

“Due diligence,” Cathcart corrected softly. “A tool. Like any tool, its morality is defined by its application. The inquiry is, of course, deniable. And slow. It would not be completed before your meeting with the Prime Minister.”

“So it’s useless.”

“It is a seed. One plants seeds with a future harvest in mind, not tomorrow’s breakfast.” Cathcart stood, leaving the thin folder on the chair. “I shall leave you to consider the illumination. The PM’s private office will call at three to confirm the pre-meeting chat tomorrow. I advise a non-committal welcome. Enthusiasm can be calibrated later.”

He left with the same silent precision. MacAulay was alone with the two folders. One blue and thick, a weapon aimed at his government’s heart. One grey and thin, a potential weapon of his own, slow-growing and poisonous.

He sat. He read the blue folder in its entirety. The complexity was the point. It was designed to be daunting, to make surrender to the summary seem rational. The numbers were beautiful, logical, and entirely fictional. They promised a world of green energy and shared prosperity. They mandated a death by a thousand contractual cuts.

His encrypted phone buzzed. Not the government one. The other. The one from the card.

A single line. *A friend suggests you walk in the Royal Botanic Garden this afternoon. The Alpine House. 3 p.m. The air is clear there.*

He deleted the message. He looked from the blue folder to the grey one. Two levers. Two forms of corruption. One was offered by the state, polished and legal. The other was offered from the shadows, raw and deniable. To resist the first, he would have to grasp the second. The ground was not just giving way; it was demanding he choose which crevice to fall into.

He closed the blue folder. He placed the thin grey folder inside his desk drawer. He locked it.

Then he put on his coat and left, telling his aide he was going for an airing. The frost had melted, as he knew it would. The streets were left damp and sullied.

*

The Royal Botanic Garden was a world of sleeping potential. The trees were skeletal, the borders bare. Winter had pared everything back to its essence. MacAulay walked without hurry, his hands deep in his coat pockets. He saw no one watching him, which meant they were good. Or he was wrong, and this was merely a walk.

The Alpine House was a glass dome, a bubble of controlled climate. He pushed through the heavy door, and the warm, dry air of a mountain summer enveloped him. It was a shock, like stepping into a memory. The air *was* clear—thin, sharp, scented with dust and stone and tiny, tenacious blooms. He was alone among the miniature landscapes, the careful arrangements of rock and hardy, low-growing plants that survived at the edge of the world.

He did not have to wait long.

“They favour extremes, these plants.” The voice came from behind a tall rockery display. It was a man’s voice, educated, with a faint, unplaceable accent. “What would kill others is their necessity. The poor soil. The searing sun. The brutal cold.”

A man stepped into view. He was in his late fifties, dressed in a nondescript waxed jacket and corduroy trousers. He had the lean, weathered face of a hillwalker, but his eyes were the colour of flint, and they missed nothing. He held a small, open notebook, as if studying the specimens.

“I’m told you’re a friend,” MacAulay said, not moving.

“I am a friend to a cause. That may, at times, align with friendship to a person.” The man closed his notebook. “You were visited last night. You listened. That is a beginning.”

“It was a monologue. I was given no terms, no structure.”

“Structure implies an institution. We are not that. We are a… confluence. Of interests. Of grievances. Of capabilities.” The man gestured to a small, star-shaped yellow flower clinging to a crevice. “*Saxifraga oppositifolia*. Purple saxifrage. It blooms on the summit of Ben Nevis. It cracks rock to root. A gentle, persistent force.”

“I’m not interested in botany.”

“But you are. You are surrounded by delicate, hothouse flowers. The Westminster system. The Whitehall mandarinate. They require rich feed, constant, temperate care. They cannot survive where this can.” The man’s flint eyes fixed on MacAulay. “You have been shown the embrace. The blue folder.”

MacAulay’s blood went still. He showed nothing. “What of it?”

“It is the perfected form of their power. Economic, legal, polite. It strangles without a mark. To refuse it requires a strength they do not understand. A strength that can grow in poor soil.” The man took a step closer, his voice dropping, blending with the gentle hum of the climate system. “We can provide the leverage you need to refuse it.”

“The grey folder.”

A slight, acknowledging tilt of the head. “A seed. We planted it. We can make it grow. Faster than Sir Jeremy believes possible. The connections, the financial flows, the names. We have them. Not suspicions. Proof. Drip-fed into the right channels—a financial journalist here, a select committee clerk there—it would create a stink around Project Trident that would make it untouchable. The PM would have to drop it or be splattered.”

“And the cost?”

“Your public, principled opposition to the project. A fiery speech in the Chamber. A leak of your ‘deep concerns’ to the press. You become the man who said no to Westminster’s poisoned chalice. Your popularity soars. Your leverage for the devolution review increases exponentially.”

“And I become your figurehead.”

“You become your own man. Using tools we provide. The alternative is to take the folder, sign the agreement, and slowly bleed your country into a vassal state of debt and dependency. A managerial death.” The man’s smile was thin, without warmth. “You are a man who understands the weight of a stone, First Minister. Some stones are for building walls. Some are for breaking windows. You must now decide which kind of mason you are.”

MacAulay looked at the saxifrage, its improbable bloom in this fabricated alpine air. It was a trick. A beautiful, resilient trick. “You want a rupture.”

“We want a shift. Ruptures are messy. But sometimes, a crack is necessary for new growth. Your meeting tomorrow with the Prime Minister. Go. Listen. Gauge the depth of the embrace. Then decide. If you wish to act, leave your government phone in your car when you next visit your constituency office. The signal will be taken as assent. The first leak will appear within twelve hours.”

The man gave a curt nod, turned, and walked away down the gravel path, stopping to apparently examine another plant. He did not look back.

MacAulay stood in the artificial warmth. He felt the two folders, one on his desk, one in his drawer, as physical weights inside him. He felt the stone from the dyke in his hand. The clean decision.

There was no clean decision. There was only the choice of corruption: the slow, legal, suffocating kind, or the sharp, hidden, explosive kind. One would kill his ambitions with kindness. The other might kill his soul with necessity.

He left the Alpine House. The outside air was a slap of cold, wet reality. He walked back through the dormant garden, the sleeping earth, towards the city and the desk that awaited him.

*

The call from the Prime Minister’s office came at three-fifteen. Sir Jeremy took it in MacAulay’s presence, on speakerphone. The Principal Private Secretary’s voice was creamy with collegiality.

“First Minister, good afternoon. The PM is so looking forward to your chat tomorrow. He’s cleared the deck for a proper, wide-ranging discussion. The future of the Union, all that. He’s asked me to mention he’s had the kitchen prepare some of that excellent shortbread you liked last time.”

“How thoughtful of him,” MacAulay said, his voice flat.

“He is a thoughtful man. He also wondered if you’d had a moment to glance at the energy proposal his team sent over? The North Sea Grid? He’s terribly excited about it.”

“I’ve seen the outline,” MacAulay said.

“Splendid. He’ll be eager to hear your initial thoughts. Eleven o’clock then? In the Cabinet Room?”

“I’ll be there.”

The call ended. Cathcart exhaled slowly. “The shortbread is a threat. He only breaks it out when he expects compliance.”

“I know.”

“Your tone was… correct. Non-committal. The folder?”

“Is a masterpiece of malign ingenuity.”

“And your inclination?”

MacAulay looked at the grey sky through the window. “My inclination is to throw the whole damn thing in the Thames.”

“A cathartic impulse, but not a policy.” Cathcart gathered his papers. “I will draft two sets of talking points for you. One, a framework for cautious engagement with the project. Two, a list of technical concerns that could justify delay. The latter will be feeble. The structure is designed to override them.”

“Do it.”

When Cathcart was gone, MacAulay unlocked his drawer. He took out the thin grey folder. He opened it. It contained a single sheet of paper with a list of company names, arrows drawn between them in black ink. At the centre, circled, was a name: *Vulcan Holdings*. And a handwritten note, not in Cathcart’s script: *Ask about the fire in the Gibraltar data vault. 2019.*

He closed the folder. He had a name. He had a direction. It was a stone. He could feel its heft.

*

The evening found him in his Edinburgh flat, not the cottage. The city lights glittered, cold and distant. He poured a whisky, not to taste it, but to feel the burn. He thought of the woman in the safe house, her calm, absolute certainty. He thought of the man in the garden, his flint eyes and his talk of cracks and growth. He thought of Sir Jeremy Cathcart, a man navigating by a star only he could see.

He thought of the Prime Minister, with his shortbread and his smile and his legal, lethal embrace.

His government phone buzzed. A reminder for tomorrow. *Meeting: PM. Cabinet Room. 11 a.m.*

His other phone, the secret one, was silent. A blank slate. A choice unmade.

He finished the whisky. The decision was not about morality. It was about anatomy. What could you live without? A limb? A principle? A clean conscience?

He went to the small balcony. The night was clear now, the stars visible between the rushing clouds. Pitiless, as they had been over the safe house. He had agreed to test the ground. Now the test was here. It was not a question of whether the ground would hold, but which piece of it he would choose to stand on as it gave way.

The hook for the next chapter was not a countdown. It was a man, standing in the Cabinet Room, smelling shortbread and ambition, with a stone in each pocket, waiting to see which one he would throw.



---

# Chapter 16

## **CHAPTER 16**

The Cabinet Room smelled of beeswax, old paper, and a faint, cloying note of vanilla. Shortbread. It sat on a silver tray beside the Prime Minister’s elbow, a prop of homely artifice. Alistair MacAulay stood just inside the door, letting the atmosphere settle on him like dust. He had arrived early, a calculated move. To be found in place was a form of possession.

The room was a theatre of subdued power. The long coffin of polished mahogany, the red leather chairs, the portraits of men who had won wars and lost empires staring blankly down. The light was London light, filtered through tall windows and centuries of precedent, falling in pale slabs on the empty chair at the table’s head. MacAulay’s own chair, halfway down on the left, seemed a continent away from it. He did not sit. He moved to the window, looking down into the garden. The drizzle had gone. The sky was a hard, high grey, the colour of a gun barrel.

He felt the two stones in his pockets. Not literal stones. The heft of them was psychic, a balanced pressure against each thigh. In his right pocket, the list from Cathcart: the engagement strategy, the technical obfuscations, the path of sanctioned delay. A legal, bureaucratic stone, smoothed by procedure. In his left, the weight of the grey folder and its single, incendiary page. *Vulcan Holdings. The fire in the Gibraltar data vault.* A jagged thing, all edges and fracture lines.

The door opened. He did not turn.

“First Minister. Admiring the view?” The Prime Minister’s voice was a warm, confident instrument. It filled spaces without seeming to try.

“Admiring the lack of it, Prime Minister,” MacAulay said, turning. “From my office in Edinburgh, I can see the Castle. It reminds me why I’m there. This…” He gestured to the manicured lawn, the empty benches. “This reminds me why I’m here.”

The Prime Minister, Charles Haddon, was a man who wore ease like a second skin. He was in shirtsleeves, his tie slightly loosened, an avatar of busy, approachable authority. He carried two files, which he placed on the table with a soft thump. “A necessary chore, I’m afraid. The price of union.” He smiled, indicating the shortbread. “I had them bring that in for you. A taste of home.”

“Very thoughtful.” MacAulay did not move towards it. The vanilla scent was stronger now, almost medicinal.

Haddon took his seat at the head of the table, not the Prime Minister’s usual chair, but one adjacent, a subtle gesture towards informality. He gestured for MacAulay to take the seat opposite. It was not MacAulay’s assigned chair. It was closer. The geometry of the room shifted, became intimate, a tête-à-tête masquerading as a state meeting.

MacAulay sat. The leather sighed beneath him, cold through the wool of his trousers.

“Alistair,” Haddon began, leaning forward, elbows on the table. The use of the Christian name was a velvet glove. “This devolution review. It’s a circus. You know it, I know it. A parade of grievances for the cameras. But the substance…” He tapped one of the files. “The substance is the North Sea Grid. Your pet project. Our shared opportunity.”

“It’s a piece of critical national infrastructure,” MacAulay corrected, his tone mild. “Not a pet.”

“Of course. Poor phrasing.” Haddon’s smile was unruffled. “The point is, its success is paramount. For Scotland’s energy future, for the UK’s security. The review’s terms of reference could… complicate the approval process. Introduce regional assemblies, new planning layers. Delay upon delay.” He spread his hands. “I want to cut through that. I want to offer you a fast-track. Whitehall-led, Treasury-backed, planning hurdles pre-cleared. Get the spades in the ground within eighteen months.”

The offer hung in the beeswax air, generous and immense. It was everything his government had lobbied for. It was Cathcart’s first stone, polished and offered back to him on a silver tray. MacAulay felt the weight in his right pocket grow denser, more legitimate.

“That’s a significant intervention,” MacAulay said. “What’s the price of the fast-track?”

Haddon’s eyes, a pleasant, forgettable blue, held his. “Coordination. Not a price, a partnership. The grid will be a UK asset. Its governance should reflect that. A joint oversight board. Westminster and Holyrood, yes, but with a casting vote residing here, on operational security matters.” He paused, letting the shape of the thing settle. “It’s a formality, really. A technicality. But it ensures nothing can be… weaponised. By either side.”

*Weaponised.* The word was carefully chosen. It spoke of suspicion, of a future where cooperation curdled. It made the casting vote sound like a sensible safeguard, not a veto.

“You’re asking me to cede sovereignty over a network built on Scottish wind, Scottish seabed,” MacAulay said, no inflection in his voice.

“I’m asking you to protect it,” Haddon countered smoothly. “From political winds. From your own hardliners who’d see it fail if they couldn’t wholly own it. This way, it gets built. Scotland gets the jobs, the energy, the investment. The glory is shared, but the prize is secured.” He leaned back. “It’s the adult solution, Alistair.”

The adult solution. MacAulay looked past Haddon, at the portrait of Pitt the Younger, a man who had spent his life holding a fracturing kingdom together. The face was pale, strained. There was no adult solution, only choices between kinds of failure.

“And the review?” MacAulay asked.

“The terms will be drafted… generously. A focus on cultural and educational devolution. The meaty economic levers, the union-critical ones, will be left for another day. A quieter day.” Haddon’s meaning was clear. *Kick the can. Take the win.*

MacAulay’s hand brushed his right pocket. The path was clear. He could agree in principle today. The details would be worked out by Cathcart and his counterparts. He would return to Edinburgh a victor, the man who delivered the grid. The price would be buried in annexes, a slow leak of authority that would only matter in a crisis. A crisis he might never see.

He thought of the woman in the safe house. *They create a weakness, then they offer to be your crutch.* He thought of the man in the garden. *The crack is where the light gets in. And the infection.*

“It’s a compelling offer, Prime Minister,” MacAulay said, his voice a dry, considered thing. “But I’ve been reviewing the consortium behind the main construction contract. Vulcan Holdings. Their structure is… opaque.”

A flicker, so swift it might have been a trick of the grey light, passed behind Haddon’s eyes. It was not surprise. It was recognition, quickly veiled. “Vulcan are solid. Financially robust. They’ve done major projects in the Gulf, in Asia. A UK-led consortium needs that global heft.”

“Heft is one thing,” MacAulay said, drawing the words out. “Transparency is another. There was an incident. A data vault fire. In Gibraltar. 2019. Wiped a significant number of their subsidiary records. Convenient timing, given the regulatory inquiries into offshore contracting in the region.”

The room was very quiet. The distant hum of Whitehall traffic was a world away. Haddon’s face had not changed, but the ease had solidified into something else, something like porcelain.

“Where did you hear about a vault fire?”

“The wind blows all sorts of debris into a First Minister’s office,” MacAulay said. “I make it a point to look at the debris. This particular piece… it suggests Vulcan’s robustness might be built on a foundation of conveniently missing paperwork. I’d be negligent to fast-track a project of this scale with that cloud hanging over the lead partner. Unless, of course, there’s a reassurance I’m missing.”

It was a throw. Not the full, jagged stone, but a chip of it. A test of the ground.

Haddon studied him for a long moment. The friendly pretence evaporated, not with anger, but with a slow, chilling precision. He reached for the shortbread, broke a piece off, but did not eat it. He crumbled it between his fingers on the polished wood.

“Alistair,” he said, and the name now was a tool, not a gift. “Governance is about seeing the big picture. Not every piece of debris is relevant. Sometimes, debris is just… mess. And in pursuit of a great national project, sometimes we have to tolerate a degree of mess. The alternative is paralysis. And paralysis is a luxury a united kingdom cannot afford.”

“Is that a reassurance?”

“It is the only reassurance you will get.” The words were flat, final. “Vulcan is the partner. The oversight board is the condition. The fast-track is the prize. This is the package. It is not a buffet from which you may pick and choose.”

The velvet glove was off. The architecture of the offer was laid bare: it was a single edifice. You took it whole, with its hidden rooms and its locked doors, or you did not take it at all.

MacAulay felt the two stones, left and right. The smooth one had just revealed its true nature: it was part of the same geology as the jagged one. The fast-track, the oversight, Vulcan Holdings—they were all facets of the same control. Accepting one meant accepting it all. The corruption was not a flaw in the offer; it was its binding agent.

He saw, with a cold, clarifying certainty, what Cathcart’s role was. The Permanent Secretary was not navigating by his own secret star. He was the cartographer, drawing MacAulay a map that led to this room, to this choice, making the inevitable seem like a series of sensible, incremental decisions. *Test the ground.* The ground was quicksand.

He stood up. Haddon, still seated, looked up at him, a faint line of irritation between his brows.

“This is not a negotiation, Prime Minister,” MacAulay said. “It is an ultimatum. I don’t accept ultimatums before lunch.”

“Sit down, First Minister. This is not the time for theatre.”

“It’s the only time for it.” MacAulay pushed his chair in, the legs scraping softly on the parquet. “You’ve presented your package. I will consider it. But I will consider it alongside the Gibraltar fire, and the structure of Vulcan Holdings, and the question of why a project of ‘national’ importance requires a casting vote from one parliament over another. I will consider the debris.”

Haddon’s face hardened. The porcelain mask was gone, replaced by the granite beneath. “Be very careful. You are playing with a live wire. That grid is your legacy. Without it, you are a footnote. A man who talked about windmills.”

“Maybe,” MacAulay said, moving towards the door. “But I’d rather be a footnote who could read the small print than a chapter heading in someone else’s book.”

He left the room, closing the door softly on the smell of beeswax and crumbling shortbread. The private secretary in the outer office looked up, startled. MacAulay did not speak. He walked down the corridor, his footsteps echoing in the marbled silence, the two stones in his pockets now feeling not like choices, but like a single, intolerable burden.

*

He did not return to his Whitehall flat. He told his driver to take him to King’s Cross. He bought a ticket for the next train to Edinburgh with his own credit card, a mundane, traceable act that felt, perversely, like a secret. He needed the three hours of suspension, the limbo of the journey, the anonymous thrum of the train.

He sat in a standard-class window seat, watching the London sprawl give way to fields still winter-bare. The meeting replayed in his mind, not in words, but in sensations: the cloying vanilla, the cold leather, the crumble of shortbread on dark wood. Haddon’s shift from warmth to granite. It was the revelation of a boundary. The Prime Minister had expected a negotiation over the size of the leash. He had not expected the leash itself to be questioned.

His government phone buzzed once, a message from Cathcart. *Hope the meeting was productive. Shall we discuss outcomes this evening?* The neutrality of it was a masterpiece. MacAulay did not reply.

The secret phone remained silent. A blank slate. He took it out, held it in his palm. It was light, a dead weight. He had thrown a chip of stone in the Cabinet Room. It was time to throw the whole thing, or to walk away from the cliff edge.

He typed a message, not to the woman, but to the number that had sent the coordinates of the safe house. *Gibraltar fire confirmed as pressure point. Haddon reacted. What is Vulcan’s true stake?*

He sent it. The act was irrevocable. He was no longer testing the ground. He was digging in it.

The reply came forty minutes later, as the train sped through the rain-smeared outskirts of Newcastle. *Vulcan is a conduit. Not a stakeholder. The stake belongs to a private pension fund for senior Crown service. Fire erased the trail of fee extraction. The grid guarantees the fund’s yield for 30 years. Your oversight board protects the conduit.*

MacAulay read it three times. The cold in his gut was not shock, but confirmation. It was not about energy, or sovereignty, or even political advantage. It was a pension scheme. A golden, unbreakable, thirty-year annuity for the mandarins and the ministers, funded by Scottish wind and guaranteed by the British state. The corruption was so vast, so bureaucratic, it was almost elegant. It wasn’t a backroom bribe; it was a structural inevitability. The grid would be built to serve a ledger. The nation was a side-effect.

He looked at his reflection in the darkening window. He saw the face of a man who had just understood the true anatomy of power. It was not a beast of passion and conspiracy. It was a self-perpetuating mechanism, a slow, patient engine that converted principle into yield, and dissent into debris. What could you live without? They had learned to live without conscience. It was the one limb the mechanism had surgically, collectively removed.

*

His Edinburgh flat was dark, filled with the city’s metallic night chill. He did not turn on the main lights. He poured a whisky, a full measure, and drank it standing by the balcony door. The burn was not cleansing; it was an accent, a highlight to the bitterness.

He had a name. He had a direction. He had a stone of undeniable heft. And he had nowhere to throw it that would not bury him in the rubble.

If he exposed it, using the secret channel, it would be denied. Vulcan would produce new, clean paperwork. The pension fund would be a patriotic investment vehicle. He would be the nationalist conspiracy theorist, sabotaging a national project for political gain. Haddon would be ‘disappointed’, the fast-track would vanish, the grid would be delayed for a decade, and he would be the man who killed it. His career would be over, and the mechanism would churn on, slightly adjusted, mildly inconvenienced.

If he accepted the package, he became part of the conduit. He would be the respectable face on the prospectus. He would deliver the grid, and his legacy would be a power station feeding a bank account.

If he walked away, resigned, he would be a martyr to a cause no one would understand. A footnote about principles in a world that traded only in outcomes.

He was trapped in the Cabinet Room’s geometry still, every path leading back to a version of defeat.

The secret phone buzzed again. A new message. *The crack is not in the deal. It is in the people who broker it. Cathcart’s knighthood was gazetted six months after the Gibraltar fire. For ‘services to public/private finance’. The honour is not a reward. It is a receipt.*

MacAulay closed his eyes. The man in the garden. *Find the crack.* They were not offering him a crack. They were showing him that Cathcart *was* the crack. The loyal servant, the neutral guide, was a paid component. A receipt had been issued.

He thought of Cathcart arranging invisible lint, speaking of overrides and technical concerns. A man navigating by a star called a pension fund.

A plan, cold and ruthless, began to form. It was not a moral plan. It was an anatomical one. It involved using one part of the mechanism against another. It involved not throwing his stone at the edifice, but placing it carefully, precisely, inside the gears.

He went to his desk. He opened his laptop. He began to draft two letters. The first was to the Prime Minister, a model of constructive ambiguity. *Thank you for the frank discussion. My officials will engage on the oversight board model, with a focus on clarifying the casting vote mechanism. I remain committed to the fast-track.* It was a holding move. It would keep the door open, keep Cathcart in the game.

The second letter was to the Presiding Officer of the Scottish Parliament. He requested an emergency ministerial statement in two days’ time, on the topic of ‘Transparency in Major Public Contracts’. The title was a bland shield. The content was not yet written.

He saved both drafts. He did not send them.

He took out the grey folder. He looked at the handwritten note. *Ask about the fire.* He had asked. Now he had the answer. He needed the proof. Not the evidence, which could be forged or lost, but the testimony. The crack had to speak.

He picked up the secret phone. His message was short. *I need to understand the nature of the receipt. Can the source be approached?*

The reply was almost immediate. *High risk. Source is insulated. But pressure on the conduit may cause a leak. The oversight board talks are your pressure point. A reluctant signatory creates uncertainty. Uncertainty frightens investors who expect certainty.*

MacAulay understood. He had to become the reluctant signatory. He had to drag the talks out, introduce his own feigned concerns, his own technical obfuscations. He had to make the guaranteed yield look less guaranteed. He had to make Cathcart, the broker, sweat. A sweating man might make a mistake. A sweating man might need to reassure his own masters. A sweating man might, if pushed to the brink, seek a lifeline from another direction.

He would have to dance with Cathcart, closer and closer, a waltz of mutual suspicion, until one of them slipped.

He finished the whisky. The decision was made. It was not about morality, or legacy, or clean conscience. It was about surgery. He would operate on the mechanism from the inside, using its own tools, hoping to sever one corrupted limb without the patient bleeding out on the table.

He went to the balcony. The night was clear and pitiless, the stars sharp as diamond points. Down in the New Town, a police siren wailed, a tiny human sound against the cosmic silence. He was no longer a man with a stone in his pocket. He was a man who had swallowed the stone, letting its weight anchor him in the dark tide. He would become the uncertainty. He would become the risk.

The hook for the next chapter was not a countdown, or a meeting. It was a conversation over a perfectly poured gin and tonic in a private club, where Sir Jeremy Cathcart would smile his neutral smile and ask, with genuine professional curiosity, what it would take to get the First Minister’s signature. And Alistair MacAulay would begin to lie, beautifully and precisely, about the price of his soul.



---

# Chapter 17

## **CHAPTER 17**

The gin was not Scottish either. It was a London gin, distilled to a point of tasteless purity, served in a chilled glass at a table that felt adrift in the amber gloom of the club. The leather was the colour of old blood, the wood dark with the patina of a century’s worth of murmured confidences. Sir Jeremy Cathcart stirred his own drink, the ice tinkling a single, precise note. The sound was a full stop in the quiet.

“I must say, First Minister, your office was rather cryptic about the agenda for our little drink. ‘Clarification on the oversight board’s remit.’ It sounded suspiciously like work.” Cathcart’s smile was a professional tool, deployed to put the other person at ease by acknowledging the absurdity of the pretence. They were both here to work.

Alistair MacAulay took a slow sip. The gin was cold, astringent, a surgical spirit. “Everything is work, Jeremy. Even this. Especially this. I find the proposed remit… problematically broad.”

“Broad?” Cathcart’s head tilted a degree, the polished neutrality giving way to a flicker of genuine professional interest. A problem to be defined was a problem to be solved. “The memorandum was quite specific. The board oversees the operational integrity of the fund. It ensures the guaranteed yield mechanism functions without political interference. A firewall. You were, I recall, adamant about a firewall.”

“Aye. A firewall. Not a bricked-up window.” MacAulay set his glass down on a brass coaster, the sound a soft *thud* in the muffled room. “Your draft gives the board unilateral audit powers over any Scottish Government expenditure with even a tangential link to the fund’s underlying assets. That’s not oversight. That’s a veto dressed in a sub-clause. You could paralyse a hospital procurement if you argued the construction firm was part of a pension portfolio three holding companies removed.”

Cathcart’s smile didn’t waver, but it cooled, like a lamp turned down. “Transparency requires light, First Minister. And light, by its nature, spreads. The investors’ concern is systemic risk. A bad contract here, a corner cut there… it can undermine confidence in the entire architecture. They are buying certainty. We are selling it. Certainty is expensive. It requires… comprehensive assurance.”

MacAulay leaned back, letting the old leather sigh around him. He was a man with a stone in his gut, and its weight made him still. “Then we have a different understanding of the product. I thought we were selling a financial instrument. Not a sovereignty instrument.”

The word hung in the air between them: *sovereignty*. It was the stone he had thrown into the still pool of their negotiation. Cathcart’s eyes dropped to his glass, as if reading the future in the melting ice. “That is a provocative term.”

“It’s an accurate one. You’re not asking for a seat at the table, Jeremy. You’re asking for the right to inspect the kitchen, interview the chef, and approve the menu before we’re allowed to feed ourselves. That’s not a partnership. It’s a receivership.”

Cathcart took a long, slow drink. When he looked up, the professional curiosity was back, sharper. “What would make it palatable?”

This was the moment. The beginning of the lie. It had to be beautiful, precise, and just selfish enough to be believable. It had to be a price for his soul that a man like Cathcart could understand.

MacAulay let a silence build, a show of reluctant calculation. He traced a finger through the condensation on his glass. “The audit trigger,” he said, his voice low, almost confessional. “It can’t be unilateral. A dual-key mechanism. A concern is raised by the board, but an investigation requires the sign-off of the Scottish Finance Secretary. A Scottish minister retains the final say on what constitutes a legitimate inquiry into Scottish governance.”

Cathcart’s expression was a masterclass in quiet dismay. “That reintroduces the political interference the firewall is designed to prevent.”

“It reintroduces *accountability*,” MacAulay countered, his tone hardening just a fraction. “To the parliament in Edinburgh, not just the boardroom in Mayfair. Without that, you’re asking me to sign a document that makes me a viceroy, not a First Minister. My political opponents would fillet me. And rightly so.” He paused, letting the political reality sink in. “The guaranteed yield is a crown jewel. But if the price is wearing the crown while someone else holds the sword, the transaction becomes… unwise. For me.”

There it was. Not principle, but prudence. Not national interest, but political survival. A language Cathcart spoke fluently.

Cathcart steepled his fingers, his gaze turning inward, assessing. “A dual-key. It creates delay. Uncertainty.”

“It creates due process,” MacAulay said softly. “A small delay for a great deal of political cover. My signature becomes… obtainable. For that.”

“And the composition of the board itself?” Cathcart asked, moving to the next point, testing the boundaries of the negotiation. “The draft specifies two members appointed by the investor consortium, one by the UK Treasury, one by the Scottish Government.”

“I want the Scottish appointment to be the chair,” MacAulay said, the demand landing with a quiet thud.

“Impossible.”
“Then make them co-chairs. With the casting vote residing in the… independent member.” He waved a hand, a gesture of apparent concession. “The one appointed by mutual consent. The one who is, presumably, actually independent.”

Cathcart’s eyes narrowed a fraction. He was recalculating. The First Minister was not refusing to sell; he was haggling over the protections built into his own servitude. This was familiar ground. This was manageable. “The casting vote is a significant lever.”

“It’s a face-saving device,” MacAulay sighed, as if weary of the game. “A symbol. The real power is in the audit. Give me the dual-key on the audit, and the chairmanship is a theatre we can argue about later. I need something to take back to my cabinet, Jeremy. Something that looks like a win. Or this…” He gestured between them with his glass. “This all becomes academic. The talks stall. The review is noted. The fund remains a theoretical model. And your investors’ certainty evaporates before the ink is dry on their prospectus.”

He saw it then, the first faint sheen of professional concern on Cathcart’s imperturbable surface. Not sweat, not yet. But a tightening. A man whose entire purpose was the smooth facilitation of a done deal, facing the spectre of friction.

“You’re introducing conditionality where none was envisaged,” Cathcart said, his voice a degree cooler.

“I’m introducing reality,” MacAulay replied. “You’ve dealt with devolution before. You know the dance. Nothing is given without a token being taken. The token here is a shred of perceived control. Give me that, and the mechanism proceeds. The yield is guaranteed. The money flows. The only thing that changes is the speed of the ink drying on my signature.”

Cathcart finished his gin and tonic. The ice settled in the empty glass. He did not signal for another. “I will need to consult. The dual-key proposal… it will be seen as a material change.”

“Consult,” MacAulay said, nodding as if he had expected nothing less. “But consult quickly. My cabinet meets on Thursday. I’d like to have something to temper the… more sceptical voices. Fergus Knox is already calling it a ‘corporate bailiff’s charter’. I need ammunition, Jeremy. Not obstacles.”

The name ‘Knox’ was another carefully placed stone. Cathcart’s file on the Finance Secretary would be thick, annotated with words like ‘unpredictable’, ‘populist’, ‘dangerous’. A plausible source of resistance that had nothing to do with MacAulay’s own hidden calculus.

“I understand,” Cathcart said, rising. He adjusted his cuffs, a tiny, automatic gesture of restoration. “I will be in touch before Thursday. This was a useful conversation. It is always better to understand the true topography of the terrain.”

They shook hands, Cathcart’s grip dry and firm. MacAulay’s was cool from the glass. No warmth passed between them.

“Aye,” MacAulay said, releasing his hand. “The terrain. Watch for the bogs.”

He waited until Cathcart’s figure had been absorbed by the gloom of the club’s hallway before sitting back down. He did not order another drink. He sat in the silence, feeling the weight inside him, the anchor he had swallowed. The lie was now in play. It had a shape, a logic. It was a feint, a demand for a procedural fig leaf that would, he hoped, make Cathcart and his masters push harder on their end, lean more heavily on their conduits, apply more pressure to make this reluctant signatory comply. Pressure caused leaks. Leaks revealed sources.

But as he sat there, a colder thought seeped in. What if his lie was not the only one on the table? What if Cathcart’s consultation was not about resistance, but about acceleration? What if the ‘material change’ was a welcome one, providing the perfect cover for something else?

His phone vibrated in his pocket. A message from Isla, his private secretary. *Security sweep of your residence is complete. All clean. One anomaly: a minor, intermittent fault detected on the landline for the last 72 hours. Engineers found no cause. Likely a glitch in the exchange.*

He stared at the words. *Intermittent fault*. A glitch. Or a listening device too sophisticated for the standard sweep to find, one that powered on and off in random bursts to avoid detection. A device that would not be planted by Cathcart’s people—they would never risk a direct breach of the First Minister’s home. That was the realm of other players. Players for whom a financial mechanism was just one piece on a larger board.

The stone in his gut felt heavier. He was not just dancing with Cathcart. The music was changing, and other dancers were joining the floor, their steps unseen in the dark. He had begun to lie to expose a truth. But the truth, it seemed, was a hall of mirrors, and his own reflection was starting to distort.

He left the club. The London night was damp, the drizzle returned, clinging to his coat like a cold sweat. He looked up at the sky, a dull orange dome of reflected light. No stars. No clean, pitiless clarity. Just the smear of a city that traded in shadows.

The hook for the next chapter was not a consultation, or a cabinet meeting. It was a report on his desk the next morning, a routine update from the government’s internal security unit. A single line buried on page four: *Following the anomaly at the First Minister’s residence, a pattern review of other ministerial and senior civil service communications was initiated. A similar intermittent fault pattern was identified in the landline records of the private office of the Permanent Secretary, Sir Jeremy Cathcart, for a matching 72-hour period.* They were both being listened to. By the same third party. Which meant the private club, the polished lies, the careful negotiation—all of it was already someone else’s script. And MacAulay had just spoken his lines perfectly.



---

# Chapter 18

## **CHAPTER 18**

The report on his desk the next morning smelled of toner and stale air. It was a bland document, a sheaf of A4 bound in a grey cover, titled *Quarterly Communications Integrity Summary*. It had the bureaucratic perfume of something designed to be ignored. MacAulay had read it standing at the window of his Whitehall office, watching the morning traffic clot along the Thames. He read it once, then again, the words not changing, only their meaning deepening, like a stone dropped into a dark pool.

*…a similar intermittent fault pattern was identified in the landline records of the private office of the Permanent Secretary, Sir Jeremy Cathcart…*

The stone in his gut, that familiar lodestar of dread, did not drop. It crystallised. It became a hard, cold fact. The private club, the brandy, the elegant dance of mutual exposure—it had all been performed on a stage they hadn’t built, under a light they hadn’t switched on. They were not two predators circling each other in a private clearing. They were specimens in a shared tank, being observed by a hand they could not see.

He placed the report down carefully, aligning its edges with the leather blotter. The victory of the previous night—the grim satisfaction of having hooked Cathcart, of forcing the man’s polished mechanism to stutter—was ash. It was worse than ash; it was a prop. He had been playing for leverage, for a sliver of control in a game he was only beginning to map. But the map was wrong. The control was an illusion. The game was several layers deeper, and he and Cathcart were not opposing players. They were pieces on the same square, their supposed conflict a distraction for the real move being made elsewhere.

Power did not just corrupt. It compartmentalised. It nested. He had been fixated on the corruption of a man, a ministry, a financial sleight-of-hand. He had missed the architecture. This was not about a single mechanism being perverted. It was about the very space in which mechanisms were built being owned. Someone was listening to the listener. Monitoring the monitor. And that changed everything.

He did not summon Cathcart. To do so would be to perform again, to speak lines into a live microphone. Instead, he did nothing. He attended the morning briefing on the devolution review, his questions neutral, his demeanour that of a man mildly preoccupied with constitutional minutiae. He watched Cathcart, who was, as ever, a masterpiece of attentive blankness. The Permanent Secretary offered a clarification on funding formulae, his voice a smooth instrument of authority. MacAulay watched his hands, the way they rested on the polished table. No tremor. No tell. Either the man did not yet know his own office was compromised, or he knew and his professionalism was a wall of sheer ice. MacAulay suspected the former. A man like Cathcart would not tolerate a breach of his own sanctum. His anger would be cold, surgical, and entirely personal. The fact he was proceeding as normal suggested the security report had not yet reached him, or had been deliberately kept from him.

Which meant the listeners were not Cathcart’s people. And they were not MacAulay’s. They were a third party, one with the reach to bypass the security of both the First Minister of Scotland and the Permanent Secretary of the Scotland Office. The list of entities with that capability was vanishingly short. And it did not include any faction with a purely financial interest.

The meeting ended. MacAulay returned to his office and closed the door. The silence was a held breath. He looked at the phone on his desk, a sleek, government-issue unit. A glitch in the exchange. He thought of the stone in his hand, the clean decision. Throw it, or don’t. There was no stone for this. Only wires, and ghosts in the wires.

He needed a voice that could not be heard on a wire. He needed a conversation that left no trace in an exchange. He needed granite and salt air.

“I’m taking an hour,” he said to his private secretary, pulling on his coat. “Personal time. I’ll be mobile if the sky falls in.”

He left the building not by the front entrance, where the press pool loitered, but through a service corridor that led to a small courtyard used for deliveries. He walked, turning without pattern, his collar up against the damp. He bought a cheap pay-as-you-go phone from a newsagent on a side street, paying with cash from his wallet, the notes damp from the London air. He walked until he found a small, dank public garden, empty save for a man feeding pigeons crusts of bread from a paper bag. He sat on a bench slick with condensation, the iron cold through his trousers.

He dialled a number he knew by heart, one that rang in a cottage looking out over the Minch.

It was answered on the third ring. “Aye?”

The voice was like a physical blow. It was his older brother, Calum. It was home. It was a world where a fault in the line was a thing to be fixed with a spanner and a curse, not a symptom of a silent war.

“Calum. It’s me.”
A pause. A crackle on the line that was just the distance. “Alistair. You’re on a bad line. Or a strange one.”
“Aye. Listen. I need you to do something. I need you to go to the old boathouse. Do you have a pen?”
“I have my head. Speak.”
MacAulay gave him a name. A London number. “Call it from the landline in the boathouse. Ask for Euan. Tell him the grouse is flying blind. Tell him I need the weather. He’ll know what it means. Then hang up. Don’t call me back. I’ll call you on this number tomorrow, same time.”
“The grouse is flying blind,” Calum repeated, his voice flat. “You’re in trouble.”
“It’s not that kind of trouble.”
“All trouble is that kind of trouble. I’ll do it.”
“Thank you.”
“Alistair.” Calum’s voice hardened. “Is it political?”
“It’s the kind of politics that doesn’t end at the ballot box.”
Another silence. He could hear the wind off the sea in the background. A clean wind. “Then watch your back. And your front. And what’s under your feet.”
The line went dead. MacAulay sat for a moment, the cheap plastic phone heavy in his hand. Euan was a relic, a friend from university who had taken a different path, into the shadowed world of signals intelligence before leaving, disillusioned and dangerously knowledgeable. He was a man who understood the ghosts in the wires. *The weather*. A request for a sense of the electronic landscape, of who might be listening, and how.

He dismantled the phone, removing the battery and the SIM card. He dropped the pieces into three separate litter bins on his walk back to Whitehall. The act felt both absurdly theatrical and utterly necessary. It was the new ritual. The new weight in the hand.

Back in his office, the official world had continued in his absence. Papers for signing. A draft statement on fisheries. An invitation to a reception at the French Embassy. The mundane machinery of state. He worked through it, his signature a steady, automatic scrawl. He was building a performance of normalcy, brick by bureaucratic brick. He was the First Minister, concerned with quotidian governance. He was not a man who had just used his brother as a dead drop.

The performance was interrupted just after four o’clock. His private secretary entered, her face carefully composed. “First Minister. Sir Jeremy Cathcart is here. He says it’s urgent, but informal.”

MacAulay felt the crystallised stone shift inside him. *He knows*. “Show him in.”

Cathcart entered, closing the door softly behind him. He did not take a seat. He stood before the desk, and for the first time, MacAulay saw a fissure in the polished neutrality. It was not in his face, which was as composed as ever, but in his stillness. It was the absolute, motionless stillness of a man containing a violent reaction.

“First Minister.” The voice was pared back, stripped of its usual lubricating courtesy. “A curious thing. My head of office security received a routine data-share from your government’s internal security unit this afternoon. A cross-referencing exercise. It contained a detail. A minor anomaly in my office landline records.”

MacAulay said nothing. He waited.

“An intermittent fault pattern,” Cathcart continued, his eyes fixed on a point just above MacAulay’s head. “Matching the one detected at your residence. For the same seventy-two-hour period.” He finally looked at MacAulay. The grey eyes were like chips of flint. “Would you care to comment?”

“You read the same report I did, Jeremy.”
“I did. And the conclusion is inescapable. We have been subject to identical, sophisticated surveillance. By a party unknown to either of us.”
“That’s one conclusion,” MacAulay said, leaning back in his chair. “The other is that one of us is lying about being surveilled. Manufacturing a pattern to cover their own tracks.”

Cathcart’s blink was slow, deliberate. “A possibility I considered. And dismissed. The technique required to generate that specific fault signature—randomised, intermittent, bypassing standard sweeps—is not commercially available. It is the hallmark of a state-level capability. Or a contractor with equivalent resources. My people do not possess it. I assume, with respect, yours do not either.”

“You assume a lot.”
“I am paid to assume correctly. This changes the calculus, First Minister.”
“Does it?” MacAulay let a note of cold anger into his voice. “Or does it just mean our private conversation last night was anything but private? That our… understanding… was brokered in a room full of unseen ears?”

For a fleeting moment, something raw flickered in Cathcart’s eyes. It was the fury of a man whose control had been violated. It was, MacAulay realised, entirely genuine. “Precisely,” Cathcart said, the word clipped. “Which renders our previous discussion not merely moot, but potentially hazardous. We were performing a pantomime of negotiation for an audience we did not invite. Our positions, our concessions, our points of leverage—all are now known. To a third party whose interests are a mystery.”

MacAulay stood up, turning to the window. The afternoon light was failing, the sky a bruised yellow. “So we stop the pantomime. We stop the dance. You came here for a reason, Jeremy. It wasn’t to compare security reports. What do you want?”

“I want to know who is in the room with us,” Cathcart said quietly. “I do not appreciate being eavesdropped upon. It is… unprofessional. And it introduces a variable that threatens the stability of the mechanism we discussed.”

*The mechanism*. The financial instrument. The thing that was to save the books and bind their fates. Even now, under this new threat, his mind went to the machinery. The corruption was not just of power, but of priority. The system’s integrity came first.

“Forget the mechanism,” MacAulay said, turning back. “It’s compromised. If they heard us discuss it, they own it. They can expose it, manipulate it, or simply use the knowledge of it to pull our strings. We can’t proceed.”

“We must proceed,” Cathcart countered, his voice regaining a sliver of its steel. “But on a different basis. A defensive one. If we are both targets of the same external surveillance, then our interests, for the moment, align. We have a common adversary. We need to identify them. And we need to do so discreetly.”

“You’re proposing an alliance.” MacAulay almost laughed. “You and me. Against the ghosts in the wires.”
“I am proposing a temporary, tactical collaboration for mutual security. You have resources in Scotland—your security unit, your police. I have access to… other channels. Together, we might trace the source. Separately, we are blind men in a maze, and the walls are listening.”

It was a cold, logical offer. It was also a trap. To collaborate with Cathcart was to step deeper into the hall of mirrors, to trust a reflection that had already proven false. But to refuse was to remain alone in the dark, with a listener who had no face at all.

“What channels?” MacAulay asked.
“That is not something I will disclose. Not yet. Suffice to say, if this is a state-level capability, there are ways to sense the shape of it. To feel for the pressure in the dark.”
“And in return?”
“In return, we reinstate the understanding from last night. We proceed with the financial instrument, but with added layers of encryption, dead drops, analogue protocols. We make it invisible to digital eavesdroppers. We save your budget, and we preserve my project. And we do it not as adversaries, but as partners securing a mutual asset from a common threat.”

The offer was elegantly corrupt. It used the external threat to cement their crooked alliance. It transformed their vulnerability into a new bond, stronger because it was forged in shared paranoia. This was how power truly corrupted: not with a blunt offer of evil, but with a compelling argument for necessary compromise. *Join me, or we both fall to the thing in the shadows.*

MacAulay was silent for a long time. He let the weight of it settle. He thought of the stone. There was no throw, no don’t throw. There was only the slow, inexorable sinking.
“I’ll consider it,” he said finally. “I need to consult my own people.”
“Of course,” Cathcart said, the neutrality returning, smoothing over the fissure. “But time, as you know, is a factor. The devolution review looms. And our unseen friend is presumably still listening. I would advise a decision within twenty-four hours.”

He gave a slight, formal nod and left the room, closing the door with the same soft precision.

MacAulay sank into his chair. The performance was over. The new reality was in place. He had gone from hunting Cathcart, to being hunted with him. The power he sought to expose had just enveloped him, offering protection from a greater, darker power. It was a Russian doll of corruption, each layer revealing a smaller, more intricate version of the same malevolent design.

He waited until the building was quiet, the civil service day drained away. He used the newly-swept landline on his desk to call his driver. “The residence, please.”

The car was a capsule of silence. The London night was fully formed, a sodium-lit gloom. The drizzle had ceased, leaving the streets with a sickly, reflective shine. He did not look out. He looked inward, at the chessboard that had just been upended. He and Cathcart were no longer kings. They were pawns who had just realised the game was being played on two boards at once.

At his residence, he went through the motions. He ate a meal prepared by the staff, tasting nothing. He reviewed papers. He watched the ten o’clock news, a parade of other people’s certainties. At eleven, he excused himself and went to his private study, a small, book-lined room on the first floor. He locked the door.

From a locked drawer in his desk, he took out another cheap phone, a twin to the one he had destroyed. He powered it on. He waited.

The call came at five minutes past midnight. The screen showed *Number Withheld*. He connected it.

“Weather report.” It was Euan’s voice, thinned by encryption and distance. It sounded like it was coming from the bottom of a well.
“Go ahead.”
“A low-pressure system. Extensive. Centred on the usual metropolitan areas. Sophisticated gear. Military-grade. Not ours. Not GCHQ’s standard kit. This is the good stuff. The export-controlled, deniable, ‘fell off the back of an ally’s lorry’ stuff. The pattern is consistent with a nodal intercept strategy. They’re not just listening to you. They’re mapping the network. Who you call, who calls you, the timing, the duration. Building a web. The content is almost secondary.”
“Who?”
A long, static-filled pause. “Hard to say. The signature has… echoes. A blend of techniques. Israeli precision, American scale, Russian patience. A hybrid. Private contractor, almost certainly. But one with a government’s chequebook and a blank slate for a moral code.”
“Purpose?”
“Mapping. Influence. Leverage. With this level of access, they’re not just gathering intelligence. They’re preparing the ground. For what, I can’t say. But when you own the wires, you can make people hear what you want them to hear. You can make truths disappear. You can make ghosts speak.” Another pause. “Alistair. This is deep water. You’re not just being watched. You’re being *instrumentalised*. Your connections, your authority—they’re being catalogued as potential tools. Get out of the game. However you can.”
“It’s too late for that.”
“Then God help you. The weather is set in. No break expected.” The line went dead.

MacAulay placed the phone on the desk. *Instrumentalised*. His connections. Cathcart was a connection. Their financial mechanism was a connection. The devolution review was a connection. He was a node in a web being mapped by a spider he could not see. And Cathcart’s offer—to collaborate, to use his ‘channels’—was he trying to find the spider? Or was he trying to find the web’s control terminal, so he could claim it for himself?

He thought of the line from the report. *A similar intermittent fault pattern… in the landline records of the private office of Sir Jeremy Cathcart*. What if it wasn’t a breach? What if it was a receipt? Proof of purchase. What if Cathcart, or the forces he represented, had *hired* the listeners? And the discovery of the fault was not a security lapse, but a staged reveal, a way to force this very alliance, to bind MacAulay closer under the guise of a shared threat? It was a level of duplicity that made his head swim. It was the corruption of truth itself, turning reality into a weapon.

He had no proof. Only the cold, multiplying paranoia that was the true symptom of the disease. Power corrupted by creating worlds within worlds, lies within lies, until the very act of seeking truth made you a part of the fiction.

He looked at the phone. He had twenty-four hours to give Cathcart an answer. An answer that would be heard, either by the ghosts, or by the ghost-maker.

He made his decision not out of courage, but out of a sheer, stubborn refusal to be a tool. If he was to be instrumentalised, he would be a flawed instrument. A stone in the mechanism.

He would agree to Cathcart’s alliance. He would walk into the proposed collaboration. But he would do so not to trace the source of the surveillance. He would do it to trace the source of Cathcart’s knowledge. To follow the Permanent Secretary’s ‘channels’ back to their origin. He would pretend to hunt the spider, while watching the man who claimed to be hunting beside him. He would use the alliance to expose the ally.

It was a plan built on quicksand. It required him to lie better, to trust less, to become a more perfect reflection of the corruption he fought. He would have to become a ghost in the web, a silent node broadcasting false data.

He picked up the official phone, the one that was possibly clean, possibly not. He dialled Cathcart’s private line. It was answered immediately.

“Jeremy. I’ve considered your proposal.”
“Yes?”
“I’m in. We find who’s listening. Then we proceed.”
A pause, then a sound that might have been the faintest exhalation of satisfaction. “Wise. I’ll make the initial discreet enquiries on my side. I suggest you do the same. We’ll compare notes tomorrow evening. Discreetly.”
“Where?”
“The club was compromised. We’ll need a new venue. Somewhere with no wires at all. I’ll text you a location. Burner phone.”
“Understood.”
The call ended. MacAulay sat in the quiet of the study. He had just stepped from one script into another, deeper one. He had traded the role of reluctant conspirator for the role of double agent in a play where he didn’t know the author, the audience, or the ending.

He looked at his hands, resting on the polished wood. They were clean. They had signed papers, shaken hands, lifted a glass of brandy. They had never felt less his own. The weight he carried now was not the simple heft of a stone. It was the formless, crushing pressure of the entire edifice, of every lie spoken in every panelled room, of every truth twisted into a tool. It was the weight of the machine. And he was now a cog, deliberately misaligned, waiting for the whole damned thing to tear itself apart.



---

# Chapter 19

## **CHAPTER 19**

The location Cathcart sent was a glass-walled café in the middle of the Kelvingrove Art Gallery and Museum. It was a masterstroke. The space was a cathedral of noise—the echoing clatter of cups, the shriek of children dragged past Victorian landscapes, the murmur of a hundred tourists in a dozen languages. It was a place where no electronic ear could isolate a single conversation from the cacophony. The glass walls meant they could see anyone approaching from any direction. There were no corners, no shadows, only the exposed, public glare of civic enlightenment.

MacAulay arrived early, buying a terrible coffee and taking a table beside a case of iridescent Art Nouveau jewellery. He watched the main entrance. Cathcart appeared precisely on time, a grey heron picking its way through the flock of visitors. He carried a museum guidebook and a small paper bag from the gift shop.

“First Minister.” Cathcart sat, placing the bag on the table between them. “A small gift. For your granddaughter. A stuffed Highland coo. It seemed… apt.”

MacAulay didn’t touch the bag. “Is it wired, Jeremy? Or just a symbol?”

Cathcart’s smile was thin, professional. “It is a toy. A gesture of goodwill in a venue devoid of it. Shall we?” He opened his guidebook. Inside, nestled in a hollow cut from the pages, was a slim manila folder. “My initial findings. As promised.”

MacAulay didn’t reach for it. He sipped his coffee, letting the bitterness coat his tongue. “You first.”

“Of course.” Cathcart’s voice dropped, blending into the ambient din. “The surveillance on your official line is sophisticated. Not GCHQ standard-issue. It has a… commercial flavour. The architecture suggests a private intelligence firm. One with former government cryptographers on staff. The kind hired for corporate espionage, or by political campaigns in other, less scrupulous democracies.”

“A firm,” MacAulay repeated. “Not a rival department. Not the Cabinet Office.”

“It appears not. The pattern is wrong for in-house work. Too many elegant shortcuts. Civil service work is clunky, thorough, documented. This is sleek, proprietary, deniable.”

“Who hires them?”

“That is the question.” Cathcart’s eyes scanned the room, a practiced, unhurried sweep. “The payment trail is a Russian doll of shell companies. Cayman Islands, Panama, Luxembourg. My channels hit a wall of legal privilege. But the style… it’s not aggressive. It’s not blackmail-oriented. It’s archival. They are building a library of your communications, not trying to trigger a crisis.”

“For what?”

“Leverage. The most dangerous kind. The kind you don’t use until you need to move a mountain. Someone is investing in your future, First Minister. They are buying shares in your political capital, intending to call in the debt at a moment of their choosing.” He nudged the folder an inch closer. “Your turn.”

MacAulay had prepared his lie with the care of a watchmaker. He reached into his inner pocket and withdrew a single sheet of plain paper, folded twice. He slid it across the table. “My people traced a metadata anomaly. The intercepts on my phone are being mirrored. A copy of the copy is being sent to a server cluster registered to a holding company called Palingenesis Holdings. Based in Zurich.”

He watched Cathcart’s face. The name was fiction. The detail was plausible chaff. But the concept—a second layer of surveillance, a watcher watching the watchers—was a poisoned hook. If Cathcart was the spider at the centre, he would react to the suggestion of a rival web.

Cathcart’s expression did not change. He did not blink. He simply opened the folded paper, glanced at the meaningless strings of numbers and fake routing codes, and gave a slow nod. “Interesting. A redundancy. Or a rivalry. This complicates the picture.”

“Or clarifies it,” MacAulay said. “Maybe our private firm is working for someone else. Someone who doesn’t trust their own surveillance. Someone who wants their own, private feed.”
