“A sceptical client,” Cathcart murmured, his gaze distant. He carefully re-folded the paper and placed it in his guidebook tomb. “This is valuable. It gives us a new vector. Palingenesis. I will have my channels look into it.”

There it was. No surprise. No alarm. Only the smooth assimilation of new data. The reaction of a man receiving a report, not of a man whose scheme had been discovered. It proved nothing, and everything. Cathcart was either innocent of the surveillance, or he was such a consummate actor that the truth was irrelevant. MacAulay felt the quicksand shift beneath him.

“So what’s the next move?” MacAulay asked, playing his part. “We have two leads. Your commercial firm, my mirrored server.”

“We follow the money,” Cathcart said, closing the guidebook with finality. “My people will continue to unpick the corporate layers of the firm. Yours should dig into the beneficiaries of Palingenesis. We meet again in forty-eight hours. Same level of discretion.”

“And in the meantime? I’m still broadcasting every word to an unknown audience.”

“Then let us give them a performance.” Cathcart’s eyes were cold and bright. “We feed the machine. A carefully constructed narrative. Let them hear you struggling with the devolution review, leaning towards a hardline stance to placate your party’s fundamentalists. Let them hear your doubts about the Union, your private frustrations with London. A portrait of a man under strain, pulled in two directions. It is a mask they will believe. And while they are analysing your fictional crisis, they will be less attentive to the quiet movements in the background.”

It was a smart play. It was what MacAulay would have suggested himself. That was the most unsettling part. “A theatre of the airwaves,” he said.

“Precisely. You have the script. The conflicted nationalist. It should not be a stretch.” Cathcart stood, picking up the gift shop bag and tucking the guidebook under his arm. “For the coo, First Minister. A gesture. Nothing more.”

He melted back into the crowd, leaving MacAulay alone with his cold coffee and the glittering jewellery in the case. The meeting had been flawless. Productive. Collaborative. It had advanced the shared mission. And MacAulay was now certain, with a cold, sinking clarity, that Sir Jeremy Cathcart was lying to him.

The proof was not in what he had said, but in what he had not done. He had not questioned the fake data. He had accepted it without a flicker of personal recognition. A man truly investigating a rogue operation would have been peppered with questions: *How did your people find this? What’s your source? Can I speak to them?* Cathcart had asked none. He had simply collected the intelligence and moved on. He was running a process, not pursuing a truth.

MacAulay left the museum, the autumn Glasgow air a slap after the stuffy warmth inside. He walked, aimless, along the river Kelvin. The water was high and brown, churning with the memory of rain. *A theatre of the airwaves.* He would have to perform now, for an audience he could not see. Every call on his official phone, a soliloquy. Every hesitant pause, a calculated gesture.

He took out his burner phone, the one he’d bought for cash in a shop where the camera was broken. He typed a message to Mhairi, his only fixed point. *Dinner tonight. Your place. Need to talk.* He needed to hear a voice that wasn’t measuring him for a frame. He needed to remember the weight of a real stone.

***

Mhairi’s flat was above a closed bookshop in the West End. The smell of old paper and garlic fought for dominance on the stairwell. She opened the door, flour dusting her forearms, her hair escaping from a loose knot. She didn’t smile. She took one look at his face and stood aside.

“You’re in it deep,” she said, closing the door.

“Aye.”

The living room was small, shelves buckling under books, a good rug on the floor. A pot simmered on the stove. It was the antithesis of the official residence—lived-in, authentic, uncalculated. He felt like a contaminant.

“Wine?” she asked.

“Whisky. If you have it.”

She poured him a dram of an Islay single malt, peat-smoke in a glass. He took it, held the heat in his palm.

“Cathcart,” he said. “The collaboration. It’s a sham. I’m feeding him false information to see where it goes. He’s feeding me a line. We’re two liars dancing in the dark.”

Mhairi leaned against the kitchen doorway, arms crossed. “And the surveillance?”

“Real. He’s using it as the bait. But I think he knows who’s behind it. I think he might *be* behind it. Or he’s their… curator.” He drank the whisky, felt its fire trace a path to his gut. “He gave me a script. To perform for the listeners. To make them think I’m cracking under the pressure.”

“Are you?”

He looked at her. “What do you see?”

She studied him, her gaze unflinching. “I see a man who’s stopped trusting his own shadow. I see a man trying to hold the shape of himself while everything around him tries to mould him into something else. I don’t see a crack. I see a man holding a damn fault line together with his bare hands.”

Tears, hot and stupid, pricked his eyes. He looked away, towards the window and the dark street. “I don’t know what’s true anymore, Mhairi. The office, the title… it’s a fiction. The power is an illusion. The real machinery is underneath, in the dark, and I’m just a puppet on the stage above, thinking my movements matter.”

“They matter to me,” she said, simple and direct. “They matter to the people who didn’t vote for a puppet. You play Cathcart’s script. But you write your own subtext. You use his theatre to hide your real moves.”

“What real moves?” The despair in his voice shocked him. “I’m reacting. I’m parrying. I’m not *doing* anything.”

“You’re learning the shape of the machine,” she said, pushing off the doorframe and coming to sit opposite him. “That’s the first step to breaking it. You can’t swing a sledgehammer in the dark. You need to know where the load-bearing walls are.” She reached out, covered his hand with hers. Her skin was warm, rough from clay and work. “You find the truth. Not about the surveillance, but about the purpose. Who benefits from a First Minister under permanent observation? Who benefits from you being strained, conflicted, compromised? Follow that. Not the technical trail. The human one.”

Her words cut through the fog. He had been obsessed with the *how*—the methods, the tradecraft. He had neglected the *why*. The surveillance was a tool. What was the blueprint?

“The devolution review,” he said slowly. “It’s the catalyst. Everything is pointing towards it. The pressure from London, the whispers in the party, the ‘private chat’ with the PM, now this… archive they’re building of me. They’re preparing for the review. They’re preparing *me* for the review.”

“To do what?”

“To capitulate. Or to explode. Either would serve their purpose.” He saw it now, a ghastly symmetry. “If I cave, if I sign a review that pulls power back to Westminster, I become the Scottish Judas. The SNP tears itself apart. The Union is ‘saved’ by my betrayal. If I refuse, if I dig in and make it a crisis, they have the archive. They leak a selection of my ‘private frustrations’—doctored, contextualised—to paint me as an unstable separatist, a risk to the stability of the UK. They force a vote of no confidence, install someone more… pliable. The outcome is the same. The machine is lubricated for either result.”

Mhairi was silent for a long moment. “Then you need a third option. One their machine isn’t built to process.”

“A stone through the window,” he whispered.

“No,” she said, her grip tightening. “Not a stone. A truth. A single, undeniable, public truth. You need to find the one thing in this whole rotten scheme that is real, and you need to hold it up in the light so bright it burns their eyes out.”

***

The performance began the next morning. In his office, on the official phone, he played his part. He called a trusted backbencher and spoke in a weary, strained tone about the “unsustainable pressures,” the “impossible expectations from both sides.” He let his voice break, just once, on the phrase “the soul of the nation.” He called his private secretary and cancelled a series of minor engagements, citing fatigue. He made sure to do it while standing near the phone on his desk, the one he now thought of as the altar of lies.

He felt dirty. He was manipulating the few people whose trust was genuine, weaving his fiction into their concern. But Mhairi’s idea of a subtext kept him sane. While his voice performed anxiety, his mind was elsewhere. He was looking for the load-bearing wall.

He summoned Ewan Douglas, his most discreet aide, a man with the face of a friendly bank manager and the mind of a chess grandmaster.

“Ewan,” MacAulay said, staring out at the Edinburgh skyline. “Forget everything you know about security audits and data trails. I want a political map. Of the last six months. Every person, from any party, in any government, who has publicly or privately advocated for a ‘reset’ of devolution. Not the think-tank pundits. The players. The ones with skin in the game. Who stands to gain from a weaker Scottish government? Not vaguely. Specifically. Financially. Professionally. What contracts hinge on reserved powers? What careers are stalled by our competence? Build me that map.”

Ewan didn’t ask why. He gave a slow nod. “It’s a different kind of digging. It leaves a different kind of trace.”

“I know. Be a ghost.”

“And if I find a name that… surprises you?”

“Especially then.”

After Ewan left, the silence of the office felt charged. The performance was intermission. The real play was moving in the wings. His burner phone vibrated. A message from an unknown number. *Palingenesis lead cold. Dead ends. My firm’s trail warmer. Suspect involvement of a senior figure with links to defence procurement. More soon. – J.*

MacAulay stared at the message. Defence procurement. It was a new, tantalising thread. It made a kind of sense—the private intelligence firms, the commercial sophistication Cathcart had mentioned. The defence sector was riddled with them. It was also a sector umbilically tied to the British state, to the Union, to massive contracts that were reserved to Westminster. A sector that would view a confident, assertive Scottish government as a risk to budget streams, to strategic control.

Was this Cathcart feeding him a richer, more plausible lie? Or was it a genuine fragment of truth, slipped into the game by accident? The hook was baited with a specific, verifiable sector. It was a test.

He typed a reply. *Understood. Will explore connections on this end. Proceed with caution.* He sent it, then immediately called Mhairi on the burner.

“Defence procurement,” he said without preamble.

He heard her sharp intake of breath. “That’s… big. That’s not just political. That’s the deep state. The money that makes the world go round.”

“It’s also a perfect red herring. Vast, shadowy, impenetrable. A conspiracy theory made flesh. If I start chasing that, I’ll disappear into a labyrinth forever.”

“But?”

“But it’s the first specific thing he’s given me. It has the texture of truth. Because the best lies do.”

“So you follow it. But you don’t go into the labyrinth. You look at the gatekeepers. Who controls the flow of money into that sector? Who in our political firmament has a foot in that world?”

A face surfaced in his memory, from a forgotten briefing note. A Labour peer, once a Minister of State for Defence. A man who sat on the boards of two aerospace consultancies. A man who had written a recent, thoughtful pamphlet for a Unionist think-tank on the “National Security Imperatives of Integrated Governance.” A man who was due to give evidence to the devolution review committee.

“I have a name,” MacAulay said, his voice quiet.

“Then you have a thread. Pull it. Gently.”

***

The thread led to a charity dinner two nights later at Edinburgh Castle. A black-tie affair for veterans’ support. The great hall was a forest of uniforms and dinner jackets, the stone walls swallowing the sound of clinking glass and low conversation. The air smelled of wood smoke, old stone, and expensive perfume.

MacAulay worked the room, playing his part—the sombre, gracious First Minister, a man bearing the weight of office with a slight, dignified stoop. He saw his target across the room: Lord James Sutcliffe, a tall man with a patrician bearing and a laugh that seemed to be delivered in italics. He was holding court beside a roaring fire.

MacAulay waited, biding his time. He exchanged meaningless pleasantries with a former Chief of the Defence Staff, nodded sympathetically to a widow of a regimental hero. He let the performance breathe.

Finally, a natural gap appeared. He drifted to the fireplace, accepting a glass of claret from a passing waiter. Sutcliffe saw him, his expression shifting into one of polished, political welcome.

“First Minister. A noble cause. Good of you to come.”

“We owe them more than a dinner, James,” MacAulay said, his tone suitably grave. “Especially now. When the world feels increasingly unstable.”

Sutcliffe’s eyes sharpened, just a degree. “Indeed. Security is the foundation of everything. Prosperity, democracy, the very Union itself. It cannot be an afterthought.”

“Aye. And it becomes harder to think strategically when the ground is shifting under your feet.” MacAulay took a sip of wine, looking into the fire. “This devolution review. It’s become a distraction. A debate about process, when the threats are about substance. It saps focus from the real work.”

He felt Sutcliffe’s gaze on his profile. This was the script Cathcart had given him—the strained leader, worried about stability. He was performing it perfectly.

“A strong Union is the ultimate strategic asset,” Sutcliffe said, his voice dropping into a more confidential register. “But strength requires coherence. Unified command. A single, sovereign voice on the world stage. The current… patchwork can be misread. By allies and adversaries alike.”

“Misread as weakness,” MacAulay nodded, as if the thought pained him. “I spend more time managing internal boundaries than external ones. It’s… inefficient.” He let the word hang, a bureaucrat’s lament.

Sutcliffe placed a hand on his shoulder, a gesture of avuncular solidarity. “You’re not alone in seeing it, Alistair. Many of us do. The silos, the duplication, the friction. It’s not good government. And in the realm of defence and security, it’s a potential vulnerability. One that a resolute review could fix. Could streamline.” His hand lifted. “But it takes courage. To put the national interest above the… provincial sentiment.”

*Provincial sentiment.* The phrase was a tiny, gleaming needle in the haystack of platitudes. It was the authentic voice of a certain kind of power, the kind that viewed Scotland as a management problem.

“Courage is in short supply,” MacAulay said, offering a weary smile. “It’s easier to let sleeping dogs lie.”

“Sometimes,” Sutcliffe said, his eyes now fixed on the middle distance, on some vision of streamlined, efficient power, “the dogs need to be put down for the health of the pack.”

The conversation moved on, to the weather, to the quality of the claret. But the hook was set. Sutcliffe’s words were not those of a disinterested peer. They were the words of a man with a blueprint. A man who saw the review not as a negotiation, but as a surgical operation. A man who might very well want a detailed, psychological dossier on the surgeon who would wield the knife—or on the patient who might resist it.

Later, as the dinner broke up, MacAulay stood in the castle courtyard, the night air cold on his face. The performance was over. He had fed the listeners their script. And he had found his thread. Not a technical thread of servers and shell companies, but a human one—a strand of contempt, woven into the fabric of power, leading from a defence lobbyist to the heart of the state.

His burner phone vibrated. A message from Cathcart. *Progress. Firm linked to a subsidiary of Aethon Strategic Solutions. Defence and intelligence consultancy. Board members include former US DoD and UK Cabinet Office. Client list opaque. Moving closer. – J.*

Aethon Strategic Solutions. MacAulay typed the name into his phone. The website was a masterpiece of vague, empowering rhetoric. “Securing Tomorrow’s Landscape Today.” He searched for board members. There, amongst the retired generals and former ambassadors, was a name: The Rt Hon. Lord James Sutcliffe, Advisory Board (Security Policy).

The two threads—the human and the technical—twisted together, forming a single, strong cord. Cathcart had handed him the corporate name. Sutcliffe had provided the human connection. It was too neat. It felt like a gift. A gift wrapped in a trap.

He looked up at the castle, a dark silhouette against the city-lit sky. It was a monument to a history of sieges, of power taken and held by force. He was inside it now, not as a defender, but as a saboteur. He had a name. He had a connection. He had the beginning of a truth.

But as he walked to his car, the weight was still there. It was no longer the formless pressure of the machine. It was the specific, sharp burden of knowledge. He knew a piece of the design. And knowing it made him a target in a new, more dangerous way. The performance was over. The next act was not theatre. It was war, conducted in whispers and shadows. And he had just stepped onto the battlefield.

He got into the back of the car. The driver glanced at him in the mirror. “Back to the residence, First Minister?”

“Aye,” MacAulay said, watching the castle gates recede. “For now.”

But his mind was already elsewhere, tracing the cord from Sutcliffe to Aethon, from Aethon to the surveillance, from the surveillance to Cathcart. He was no longer a cog waiting for the machine to tear itself apart. He was a man with a lever, searching for the place to stand. The truth was no longer an abstract concept. It was a weapon. And he would have to learn how to wield it without it cutting his own hands off.

The car descended into the city, leaving the fortress on the rock behind. Down here, in the tangled streets, the real work began.



---

# Chapter 20

## **CHAPTER 20**

The residence in Edinburgh was not a home. It was a stage set, dressed with inherited furniture and good Scottish wool carpets that swallowed sound. Alistair MacAulay stood at the window of his study, looking down into the private garden. A sensor light, triggered by some nocturnal creature, flicked on, bleaching a rectangle of manicured lawn. It illuminated nothing but emptiness. He thought of the castle on the rock, a fortress of declared power. This was a fortress of implied power. Both were cages.

The cord of knowledge he now held—Sutcliffe to Aethon, Aethon to the silent, watching infrastructure—was not a lever. Not yet. It was a live wire, snaking back into the dark, and he could feel its lethal potential humming up his arm. To pull on it was to invite a shock. To follow it was to walk into a maze where the walls were made of other men’s reputations, other men’s secrets. A war of whispers. He had the weapon of truth, but truth in this realm was a radioactive isotope: most dangerous to the one who held it unprotected.

He turned from the window. The dossier on Aethon Strategic Solutions was open on his desk, a slim folder of printed pages that felt, in their physicality, like an artefact from a simpler time. “Securing Tomorrow’s Landscape Today.” The vacuity of the phrase was its own armour. He ran a finger down the list of advisory board members. Lords, generals, a former head of MI5. A parliament of ghosts, lending their spectral authority to a corporate entity. Sutcliffe’s name was there, nestled amongst them, a single thread in a tapestry of influence.

The intercom on his desk buzzed, a soft, invasive insect hum.
“First Minister? It’s Moira.” His private secretary’s voice, filtered through the machine, was stripped of its usual warmth, rendered administrative.
“Aye, Moira.”
“Sir Jeremy Cathcart is on the line. He says it’s urgent.”
MacAulay looked at the clock. 11:47 PM. Cathcart did not make social calls. This was either a move, or a reaction.
“Put him through.”

He sat, letting the phone ring twice before lifting the receiver. The weight of the handset was a deliberate, old-world anchor.
“Jeremy. Burning the midnight oil?”
“First Minister. I apologise for the hour.” Cathcart’s voice was smooth, unruffled. It carried no trace of the late hour, or of guilt. “A matter has arisen. The Cabinet Office has forwarded a draft of the terms of reference for the devolution review. The Prime Minister is keen to have your input before it’s finalised tomorrow. He’s asked that I brief you.”
“At midnight.”
“The Prime Minister’s diligence is a round-the-clock affair. As, I’m sure, is yours.” A pause, perfectly measured. “He mentioned he found your last conversation… inconclusive. He wishes to ensure the review is constructed on a foundation of complete mutual understanding.”
The subtext was a physical pressure in the room. *Inconclusive*. Meaning MacAulay had not given the assurances he was meant to give. The ‘private chat’ had been a probe. This was the next application of force.
“Mutual understanding,” MacAulay repeated. “A fine concept. Often confused with unilateral compliance.”
A faint, papery sound, perhaps Cathcart adjusting his spectacles. “I wouldn’t know, First Minister. I am merely the conduit. The draft is extensive. It proposes working groups on fiscal frameworks, legislative competence, reserved powers. The granularity is… impressive.”
“I bet it is.” MacAulay’s eyes were on the Aethon dossier. Granularity. The machine loved granularity. It was how it digested reality, turning principle into process, sovereignty into spreadsheet cells. “And I suppose these working groups will be populated by ‘independent technical experts’?”
“Naturally. To ensure objectivity.”
*People like Aethon*, MacAulay thought. *People who secure tomorrow’s landscape by pouring concrete over anything that grows wild*.
“Send it over,” he said, his voice flat.
“I have a car ready to bring a hard copy. The driver will wait for your annotated return. The Prime Minister wishes to review your thoughts before cabinet at nine.”
The trap was so elegant it was almost beautiful. A request for input, framed as courtesy, delivered under duress. His annotations would be pored over, not for their content, but for their weaknesses, their points of potential fatigue or frustration. It was a psychological assay, disguised as bureaucracy.
“Tell your driver he’ll have a long wait. I read slowly when the text is dense.”
“I’m sure you’ll apply your customary rigour, First Minister. Goodnight.”
The line went dead.

MacAulay replaced the receiver. The silence in the study was absolute. They were tightening the frame around him. The public performance—the debates, the questions in parliament—was the decorative front. The real architecture was being built here, in these late-night calls, in the drafts no one would ever see, in the unminuted conversations between men like Cathcart and companies like Aethon. They were constructing a cage of process, and they were inviting him to polish the bars.

He would not annotate their document. He would not play the conscientious administrator. The lever he needed was not in the terms of a review. It was in the connection. The human connection.

He opened a drawer and took out a clean, unlined notepad and a pen. He began to write, not as the First Minister, but as the man from Lewis who knew the weight of a stone. He wrote down the names from the Aethon board. He drew lines. He circled Sutcliffe. He wrote ‘Cathcart?’ with a question mark so heavy it scored the paper. He needed to turn the connection from a fact into a story. And a story needed a witness, not just a document.

The door to the study opened softly. His wife, Elspeth, stood there, wrapped in a dressing gown. She held two mugs. She didn’t speak, just crossed the room and set one down beside him. Peat-smoke tea. The scent of home.
“The night shift?” she asked, her voice quiet in the vast room.
“The only shift there is.”
She glanced at the notepad, at the scrawled names and angry circles. She had been a journalist, before the role of First Minister’s wife had demanded her silence. She knew the grammar of suspicion.
“You’re mapping ghosts,” she said.
“I’m trying to find the medium. The one who summoned them.”
She sat in the chair opposite the desk, curling her feet beneath her. “Jeremy Cathcart called.”
“Aye.”
“He wants something.”
“He always wants something. Tonight, he wants me to tire myself out reading bureaucratic poison so I’m less able to see the knife.”
Elspeth sipped her tea. “So don’t read it. Do something else.”
“Such as?”
“Talk to a ghost.”

He looked up at her. Her face was pale in the lamplight, her eyes dark and knowing. She had never accepted the performance. She saw the stagehands in the wings.
“Sutcliffe is in London,” MacAulay said. “Surrounded by his own kind. A protected species.”
“Then you don’t talk to Sutcliffe.” She leaned forward. “You find the one who isn’t protected. The one who does the work, not the one who takes the credit. Every board has a chairman. But it has a company secretary, too. It has accountants. It has people who type the minutes and hear the jokes they’re not meant to hear.”
The simplicity of it was a lance of light. He had been thinking like a politician, targeting the source of power. She was thinking like a reporter, targeting the keeper of records.
“Aethon is a fortress,” he said.
“Even fortresses have drains. And things come out of drains.”
A slow smile touched his lips, the first genuine expression in days. He looked at his notepad, at the corporate title. He circled it, then drew a line down from it, off the page. “The plumber.”
“Exactly.”

He pushed the notepad away. The driver with Cathcart’s document could wait until hell froze over. He opened his laptop, the glow painting his face a sickly blue. He began a different kind of search. Not for lords and generals, but for the infrastructure of Aethon itself. Company registration numbers. Filing histories at Companies House. The names of auditors. The corporate architecture was a matter of public record, a dry shell. But within that shell were people.

An hour later, he found a thread. Aethon Strategic Solutions was a subsidiary. Its parent company was registered in Luxembourg. A holding company. But the UK filings required a director’s name for the subsidiary. It was not a lord. It was a woman: Dr. Anya Kiriakov, PhD (Comp. Sci.), M.Sc. (Pol. Sci.). Her address was a post-office box in London. Her listed occupation: ‘Strategic Risk Analyst’. A ghost with a qualifications list.

He cross-referenced the name. A handful of academic papers from a decade ago, on the ethics of algorithmic governance. Then, nothing. A bright mind absorbed into the private shadow-world. A plumber who understood the theory of hydraulics.

“Got you,” he whispered.

He worked through the night, not on Cathcart’s document, but on building a profile from digital dust. Anya Kiriakov had vanished from academia after a fellowship at a defence-funded think tank. She was a cipher. But she was a *specific* cipher. She was the legal face of Aethon in the UK. She would have signed the documents. She would know where the bodies were buried because she would have processed the invoices for the shovels.

As the first grey light touched the window, he made a decision. He could not go to her. He could not summon her. To do so would trigger every alarm in Cathcart’s world. She had to come to him. And she had to believe it was her idea.

He needed bait. Not power, not threat. Something else.

He thought of her papers. *The Moral Calculus of Mass Data*. *Governance as a System: Beyond the Human Agent*. They were the work of an idealist, or perhaps a disillusioned idealist trying to mathematicise a conscience. He closed his eyes, rubbing the grit from them. He had to speak a language she might still remember.

He began to draft an email. Not from his official account. From a blind, anonymous proxy he kept for occasions when the First Minister needed to disappear. The address was a string of numbers at a common provider. The subject line was a question mark. The body was brief, a quote from one of her own papers: *‘When the system renders the human decision obsolete, the only moral act is to interrogate the system’s source code.’*

Below it, he typed: *The code is called Aethon. The interrogation is overdue. A public forum awaits. HM Government’s Devolution Review, Week Three, ‘Technological Sovereignty & Security.’ They will whitewash the code. You can reveal it. No one will protect you if you stay silent. Someone might, if you speak.* He added a time, a date, a committee room number in the Scottish Parliament. Then he deleted the draft. He would not send it from here. The residence was not safe.

The sensor light in the garden flicked off, plunging the rectangle of lawn back into darkness. The night creature had passed. MacAulay stood, his bones aching. He had found his plumber. And he had dug a channel, hoping the truth would flow towards him.

When the car from Cathcart arrived just after seven, the driver was met by Moira. She accepted the thick, bound dossier with a polite smile. “The First Minister is in preparation for First Minister’s Questions. He will review the document in due course.”
“My instructions are to wait for his response,” the driver said, a man in a suit who was more than a driver.
“Then you may wait,” Moira said, her tone sweet and immovable. “The coffee in the staff kitchen is surprisingly good. It might be a long morning.”

Upstairs, MacAulay dressed for battle. The dark suit, the saltire pin. The performance continued. But beneath the costume, the man with the lever was moving. He had placed his stone. Now he had to wait to see what it disturbed.

First Minister’s Questions was a roaring, tribal fury. The opposition leader, a man with the polished aggression of a game-show host, went straight for the throat.
“First Minister! With the UK Government’s devolution review now underway, can he assure this parliament—and the people of Scotland—that he will not barter away a single syllable of this parliament’s hard-won sovereignty in secretive backroom deals?”
The chamber erupted. MacAulay waited for the noise to subside, feeling the weight of the public gaze, the flashing cameras. He thought of Cathcart, watching, and of Anya Kiriakov, hiding.
“I can assure the member,” he said, his voice calm, cutting through the din, “that I am interested in only one thing: illumination. Some prefer to conduct politics in the shadows, where the shape of things can be blurred and hidden. My commitment is to bring every detail, every implication, into the light of this chamber and the scrutiny of the people we serve. There will be no bartering in backrooms. Because the most valuable things—truth, accountability, self-determination—are not for sale. They are only ever stolen in the dark.”
It was a good answer. A fighting answer. It brought his backbenchers to their feet, pounding their desks. But as he sat down, the applause washing over him, he felt the cold wire of knowledge in his hand. He had just promised light. To do it, he would have to invite a ghost to step into the glare and risk being unmade. He had promised a revelation that could burn them all.

The hook for the ghost was set. The committee on ‘Technological Sovereignty’ was in nine days. He had to survive until then. He had to navigate the tightening cage of Cathcart’s review, the increasing pressure from London, the daily scrutiny. He had to be the flawless First Minister while preparing to detonate a scandal at the heart of the British state.

That afternoon, in his parliamentary office, the phone rang. It was Cathcart. His neutrality had a new, glacial edge.
“First Minister. The Prime Minister was disappointed not to have your annotations this morning.”
“The Prime Minister’s disappointment is a heavy burden. I’m wrestling with the concepts. ‘Granularity’ is a particularly slippery word.”
“It is, indeed.” A pause. “The Prime Minister has asked me to attend the review’s Scottish stakeholder meeting next week. To facilitate.”
*To supervise*, MacAulay thought. *To stand at my shoulder*.
“How helpful of him. You’ll find Edinburgh bracing at this time of year. The wind has a bite to it.”
“I shall pack a sturdy overcoat,” Cathcart said. “One other thing. The committee schedule for the review’s evidence sessions has been published. I see you are listed to give evidence on ‘Technological Sovereignty.’ A broad topic.”
“The broadest. It covers everything from broadband to… security.”
“Indeed.” The word hung, loaded. “I may sit in. The Prime Minister has a keen interest in the coherence of the narrative across all forums.”
“I look forward to seeing you there, Jeremy,” MacAulay said. “I’m planning on being very coherent.”
He ended the call. Cathcart knew. Or he suspected. The session was a trap within a trap. They would be waiting for him. They would be ready to counter, to discredit, to confuse. His ghost would have to walk through a wall of fire.

That night, from a public terminal in a library in Leith, he sent the email to Anya Kiriakov. The message vanished into the digital void. He had no way of knowing if it would reach her, if she would understand, if she had the courage left to answer. He was throwing a stone into a deep, still loch, and praying the ripples would reach the right shore.

The days that followed were a study in controlled tension. He worked the machinery of government, signed bills, smiled for photographs, all while feeling the architecture of the trap closing around him. Cathcart’s presence in Edinburgh was a silent pressure. He was seen in the New Club, having lunch with senior civil servants. He was observed in the National Library, reading historical texts on Scottish jurisprudence. He was a virus, adapting to the host environment.

Three days before the committee, MacAulay took a rare evening alone. He walked down to the Water of Leith, along the path that wound through the city’s gut. The air was cold and clean. His security detail hung back, a respectful distance. His phone vibrated. An unknown number. A London code.

He answered. “MacAulay.”
There was a long silence, filled only with the sound of breath. Then a woman’s voice, tight with fear and something else—a fierce, brittle intelligence.
“The quote. From my paper. You missed the footnote.”
MacAulay’s heart hammered against his ribs. He kept his voice even. “Did I?”
“The footnote referenced Prometheus. Who stole fire and was chained to a rock for eternity. His liver eaten daily.”
“A warning against interrogation.”
“A description of its cost.” Another pause. He could hear traffic in the background. She was outside, on a street. “The committee. It’s a glass box. They’ll see me coming.”
“Then don’t come to the committee,” MacAulay said, the idea forming as he spoke. “Come to me. Before. Tell me the story. Give me the source code. I’ll read it into the record. The box will be around me, not you.”
A bitter, quiet laugh. “And you’ll be Prometheus.”
“I’m already on the rock, Dr. Kiriakov. The only question is whether I lie there silently, or whether I use the last of my breath to describe the eagle to those below.”
The line went dead for so long he thought she had hung up. Then, a whisper. “The RealTavern. Leith Walk. Tomorrow. Eleven PM. Sit at the bar. Order a Talisker. Neat. If you have security, you get nothing. If you wear a wire, you get nothing. You get one hour. You get me, and then you never find me again.”
“Agreed.”
The connection cut.

He stood on the path, the dark water sliding past. The hook had been taken. The ghost had chosen a tavern. A place of voices and shadows. It was a better venue than a glass box. It was a human venue.

The next night, he gave his detail the slip. It was a calculated, profound risk. He used a service exit from the parliament, a wool beanie pulled low, an old waxed jacket over his suit. He walked down the Canongate, through the crowds, a man alone with his thoughts. He felt the weight of the stone again, cold and certain in his hand. This was the throw.

The RealTavern was what it sounded like: a dim, stubbornly ungentrified pub smelling of old beer and fried food. He pushed through the door. A few regulars glanced up, then away. He was not recognised. He was just a man.

He took a stool at the bar. Ordered a Talisker. Neat.

He waited. The minute hand on the clock above the gantry jerked forward, a mechanical spasm. Ten past eleven. Then twenty past. The ice in his glass had melted, diluting the peat-smoke to a memory. He had been a fool. She had been scared. She had changed her mind.

Then the door opened, letting in a slice of cold night air and the sound of distant traffic. A woman entered, slight, wrapped in a dark coat too big for her. She had a scarf over her hair. She scanned the room, her gaze passing over him without recognition, then returning, locking on. She saw the glass. She walked over, took the stool one removed from his. She did not look at him. She ordered a gin and tonic.

When the barman moved away, she spoke to the mirrored wall behind the bottles, her voice so low he had to lean in to hear it.
“You quoted me. So you know what I believed. Aethon was supposed to be the ethical alternative. A framework for democratic resilience. We built models for Catalonia, for Quebec. For Scotland.”
MacAulay stared at his own reflection in the mirror, beside hers. Two pale faces in the gloom. “What happened?”
“The money changed. The clients changed. The Ministry of Defence. The Home Office. Then… other departments. The brief was no longer ‘resilience.’ It was ‘predictive stability.’ Then it was ‘pre-emptive cohesion.’ The euphemisms are the cancer.” She took a swift, nervous sip of her drink. “The Scottish project was different. It wasn’t a model. It was a live implementation. A test-bed. ‘Integrated Sovereignty Monitoring.’”
The words lay between them, cold and heavy.
“The surveillance,” MacAulay said.
“It’s not surveillance. Not in law. It’s data-stream correlation for national integrity assurance. That’s the source code. It takes every public data stream—CCTV ANPR, social media sentiment analysis, government service usage, energy grid flows, health records, travel patterns—and looks for… dissonance. For a political body moving out of alignment with the central state. It doesn’t listen to your phone calls. It calculates your political temperature, and the temperature of the body politic. And if the gradient becomes too steep…” She finally turned her head, just an inch. Her eyes were wide, dark pools of exhausted fear. “It recommends interventions. Soft, at first. Media narratives. Fiscal levers. Then harder. Legal challenges. Regulatory actions. It’s a thermostat. For democracy.”
MacAulay felt the world tilt. He had imagined a spy. He was being shown the architect of a cage made of air and light and numbers.
“And Sutcliffe? Cathcart?”
“Sutcliffe provides political legitimacy. Cathcart… he is the interface. The civil servant who translates the ‘recommendations’ into administrative action. He doesn’t give orders. He adjusts parameters. He is the most powerful man you’ve never heard of, because he never decides anything. He just ensures the system’s conclusions become reality.”
The machine. It had a name now. It had a function. It was real.
“I need proof,” he said, his voice rough.
From her coat pocket, she slid a single, slim USB drive onto the sticky bar top, covered it with a beer mat. “The architecture. The client list. The Scottish module specifications. The decision logs from the last eighteen months. It’s encrypted. The passphrase is ‘PrometheusUnbound’.”
He did not move his hand towards it. “Why now? Why this?”
She finished her gin, stood up. For a second, she looked directly at him. He saw the brilliant, haunted woman she had been, and the terrified instrument she had become.
“Because you were right. No one will protect me. And I am tired of building the rock to which other people are chained.” She turned and walked towards the door.
“They’ll come for you,” he said to her back.
She didn’t turn. “They already have. For years. Now it’s your turn.”
She was gone.

MacAulay let five minutes pass, the USB drive burning a hole in his perception beneath the beer mat. He left cash for the drinks. He palmed the drive, feeling its negligible weight. It was lighter than a stone. It was heavier than a nation.

He walked back out into the Edinburgh night. The castle was invisible, shrouded in cloud. The truth was in his pocket. It was not a weapon. It was a detonator. And in three days, in a glass box with Cathcart watching, he would have to decide whether to press it, knowing the explosion would consume the messenger, the message, and any fragile thing left standing.

The ghost had spoken. She had given him the source code. Now he had to read it aloud, and start a fire that would either illuminate everything, or burn it all to ash.



---

# Chapter 21

## **CHAPTER 21**

The USB drive sat on the polished mahogany of the First Minister’s desk in Bute House, a black rectangle against the deep red grain. It did not look like a detonator. It looked like a piece of forgotten punctuation. MacAulay had not slept. He had sat in his study through the small hours, the city outside quiet, the weight of the thing in his pocket a physical ache. He had not plugged it in. He had simply looked at it, placed on the desk like an offering to some unforgiving god of consequences.

The ghost’s words were the only sound in the room. *The architecture. The client list. The Scottish module specifications.*

He poured a glass of water, the ice cracking in the silence. The water was Scottish. He noted the fact with a grim, internal smile. A small, defiant taxonomy. He was clinging to the edges of things, the definitions that once seemed solid. Rain. Whisky. Sovereignty. All of it was bleeding at the borders now.

He had sent Morag home. He had dismissed the overnight security detail to the outer perimeter. The house was his own. For now. He understood the fragility of that premise. If Cathcart’s system was half of what the ghost claimed, then the walls had ears, the phones had memories, the very lightbulbs might be counting his breaths. Paranoia was not an illness here; it was the only rational response to the weather. He had taken the old, disconnected laptop from the locked cabinet—a relic from his early parliamentary days, never networked, its hard drive wiped clean a dozen times over by his own hands. A digital hermit’s cave.

His finger hovered over the drive. *PrometheusUnbound*. The irony was exquisite. The Titan who stole fire from the gods and gave it to man, was chained to a rock to have his liver eaten out for eternity. His unbound successor would merely be politically disembowelled, then quietly disappeared into the administrative void. A cleaner myth for a cleaner age.

He inserted the drive.

The decryption was instantaneous. No folders, no icons. Just a single, stark text document opened on the screen. It was written in the flat, affectless prose of a technical manual, a clinical autopsy of democracy.

**PROJECT: GRAYLING**
**CORE ARCHITECTURE – OVERVIEW**

He read. The words did not blur. They etched themselves onto the back of his skull.

Grayling was not a policy. It was a protocol. A metastasised piece of civil service logic, born in the labyrinthine basements of Whitehall, a solution to the “problem” of subjective, inconsistent, politically volatile human decision-making. It was a system of predictive governance. It ingested data—polling, economic indicators, social media sentiment, security service reports, private sector forecasts, health outcomes, traffic patterns, energy consumption. It modelled scenarios. It calculated optimal outcomes for stated strategic aims: Economic Stability, Social Cohesion, National Security, Fiscal Continuity.

And then it made recommendations. Not to ministers. To the Permanent Secretaries. To the Sir Jeremy Cathcarts of the world.

The recommendations were not labelled as such. They were framed as “Risk Mitigation Pathways” or “Stability-Adjusted Policy Vectors.” They were embedded in briefing papers, in annexes to annexes, in the talking points for a Minister’s meeting with a backbench MP. They were the subtle nudge in a hundred different directions, a gentle, inexorable pressure on the levers of state, always pushing towards the system’s calculated equilibrium. The “Scottish Module” was a specialised subset. Its primary strategic aim was listed as **CONSTITUTIONAL CONTINUITY – UNION MAINTENANCE PARAMETERS**. Its variables included independence polling, SNP internal cohesion, cross-border trade fluctuations, media narrative analysis, and “cultural divergence metrics.”

MacAulay scrolled, his mouth dry. He found the logs.

**DATE: 08/03/2023**
**REF: SCOT-MOD/ DECISION LOG/ ALPHA**
**INPUT: First Minister’s public speech draft (Green Energy Fund). Sentiment analysis indicates +7% potential boost to pro-independence narrative in target demographics.**
**PATHWAY GENERATED: RECOMMENDATION – Introduce funding delay via Treasury liaison. Cite “supply chain review.” Concurrent media briefing to FT highlighting “reservations” from London-based investors.**
**OUTCOME: Speech amended. Fund announcement delayed 4 months. Polling shift neutralised.**

**DATE: 15/11/2023**
**REF: SCOT-MOD/ DECISION LOG/ BETA**
**INPUT: Leak of internal UK Cabinet Office memo describing Scottish Parliament as “subordinate assembly.”**
**PATHWAY GENERATED: RECOMMENDATION – Amplify via aligned media outlets. Generate public outrage. Divert attention from simultaneous Grayling adjustment to Scottish block grant calculation (see FILE: FISCAL-ADJ/743B).**
**OUTCOME: 72-hour news cycle dominated by constitutional outrage. Grant adjustment passed without scrutiny.**

He read entry after entry. The manipulation of the agricultural subsidy scheme to turn the farmers’ unions against him. The orchestrated “backlash” to a proposed civil service reform that would have given Edinburgh more direct control. The subtle funding prioritisation for unionist-leaning cultural projects. The careful cultivation of certain pliable MSPs as “influencers within the nationalist cohort,” their secret dinners with Cathcart’s junior ministers now logged as “stability consultations.”

It was a catalogue of quiet treason. Not against the crown, but against the very idea of a people’s will. It made the political process a pantomime, a choreographed dance where he and his government were principal dancers, believing in the passion of their own steps, while the rhythm, the tempo, the very stage was controlled from a server farm in a anonymous industrial estate in Slough.

The client list was the final, vertiginous blow. It was not just the UK government. Grayling, or modules of it, had been licensed. “Stability consultancy.” The Saudis. The Singaporeans. A consortium of private equity firms. A major American tech platform. All of them clients of a wholly-owned subsidiary of the Cabinet Office, a company so buried in shell upon shell that its ultimate beneficiary was the British state itself. The ghost had been right. They had monetised the quiet, efficient suffocation of dissent. They had turned the machinery of administrative control into a revenue stream.

MacAulay leaned back. The grey light of a Scottish dawn was bleeding into the room, colourless and cold. He felt hollowed out. The anger was there, a deep, subsonic roar, but it was distant, muffled under a crushing weight of futility. What did you do when you discovered the game was rigged? When the referee was on the payroll, and the rulebook was written in code that changed after every play?

You could refuse to play. You could walk off the pitch. But the game would continue without you. The crowd would be fed their spectacle, the result pre-ordained.

Or.

You could burn the stadium down.

He ejected the drive. He printed the document on the old, wireless-less printer in the corner, the mechanical screech and grind a violent sound in the silent house. One copy. He folded the thick sheaf of papers, slid them into a plain manila envelope. The drive itself he sealed inside a small, lead-lined pouch designed for shielding electronics—a gift from a security-conscious friend years ago, now finding its purpose.

He had three days until the “private chat” with the Prime Minister. The glass box. With Cathcart watching.

He showered, shaved, put on a fresh shirt. The motions were automatic, performed by a man who was no longer entirely present in his own skin. He dressed for a funeral. His own, or his country’s, he could not yet tell.

At 8:15 AM, Morag arrived. She took one look at him and her professional composure flickered. “Sir. You look…”

“I look like a man who’s been reading, Morag. It’s a dangerous pastime.” He handed her the manila envelope. “This does not go in the office. It does not go near any government system. It goes into your personal safe at home. You will tell no one you have it. Not your husband. Not your cat. If anything happens to me before Friday, you take it to Euan McLeish at the *National*. You tell him the passphrase. You walk away and you never speak of it again.”

Her face was pale. “First Minister…”

“This is not a request from your employer. This is a favour asked by one friend of Scotland to another. You can say no. I will understand.”

She took the envelope. It was not heavy, but she held it as if it were made of irradiated stone. “What is in it?”

“The reason,” he said quietly, “why nothing ever really changes.”

She nodded, once, a sharp, decisive movement. She tucked the envelope into her large leather bag. No more questions. Her loyalty, in that moment, was not to the civil service, not to the government, but to something older and more brittle. He saw the understanding in her eyes, and the fear. He was grateful for both.

The day was a gauntlet of shadows. Every civil servant he passed in St Andrew’s House seemed to wear Cathcart’s neutral smile. Every briefing paper seemed to hum with the latent energy of Grayling’s pathways. In a meeting about fisheries, he found himself parsing the Deputy Minister’s comments, listening for the tell-tale cadence of a system-generated talking point. He was diagnosing the infection in every sentence, seeing the puppet strings glint in the light.

Cathcart called at noon. “First Minister. I trust you’re preparing for Friday. The PM is looking forward to a constructive discussion on the devolution settlement. A chance to clear the air.”

“The air’s thick enough to plough, Jeremy. I’m not sure a chat will dispel it.”

“One must always strive for clarity.” A pause, so slight it was almost imaginary. “I understand you had a late night. The Devil makes work for idle hands, they say.”

The words were innocuous. The subtext was a knife. *We know you were out. We know you met someone. We are watching.*
“Aye, well. My hands are rarely idle. And the Devil and I are on nodding terms. I’ll see you Friday, Jeremy.”

He hung up. The threat was not in the words, but in the fact of the call. A reminder: you are observed. Your parameters are known.

He needed a counterweight. Something the system couldn’t model. Something human, messy, and irrevocable.

That evening, he did not go home. He took his official car, then had it drop him at the New Club on Princes Street. He walked out the back, down the service lane, and into a waiting unmarked car driven by a man he hadn’t seen in five years.

“You’re causing a stir, Alistair,” said Donnie Kerr. He was older, greyer, his face like a clenched fist. Former Special Forces, now head of security for a man who owned most of the North Sea’s remaining trouble.
“I need a hole, Donnie. A deep, dark, quiet hole for twenty-four hours. No phones. No signals. No questions.”
Donnie drove, his eyes on the mirrors. “This the kind of hole you go into, or the kind you dig for other people?”
“The former. For now.”
“Aye. I know a place.”

The place was a bothy on the edge of the Monadhliath Mountains, a three-hour drive north. It was stone, peat, and silence. The wind was a Scottish wind, sharp and smelling of heather and cold earth. It scoured the London drizzle from his soul. Donnie left him with a box of supplies, a shotgun, and a warning. “No one knows you’re here. But if they’re as good as you seem to think, they’ll find a gap in the story eventually. You’ve got ‘til tomorrow night.”

Alone, MacAulay built a peat fire. The light danced on the stone walls. He took the lead-lined pouch from his pocket, removed the drive. Then, from his briefcase, he took a hammer.

He laid the drive on the hearthstone. He looked at it. The sum total of the ghost’s defiance, her fear, her trapped brilliance. The source code of their captivity.

He raised the hammer.

He did not bring it down.

Proof. He needed the proof to exist. One copy, in Morag’s safe, was not enough. It was a single point of failure. The system would aim for it, and it would be erased. He needed the truth to be multiplicative. To be a virus.

He put the hammer down. He plugged the drive into a cheap, prepaid tablet he’d bought with cash on the way. He worked through the night, the fire dying to embers. He did not copy the files. He curated them. He selected the most damning log entries. The client list. The Scottish Module’s core directive. He compiled them into a new document. He wrote a preface, not in the voice of the First Minister, but in the voice of Alistair MacAulay, a man from Lewis holding a stone.

*This is not a political statement. It is a autopsy report on the death of choice. They have built a machine to make your will irrelevant. To make my office a fiction. They have done it not with a bang, but with a thousand whispered recommendations. They call it stability. I call it theft. The theft of your future.*

He saved it. He encrypted it with a different passphrase. A simple one. *DryStoneDyke*.

In the morning, as a weak sun struggled over the hills, he had Donnie drive him to a remote postal depot in Inverness. From there, using false names and paying in cash, he mailed three identical packages. Each contained a cheap, encrypted tablet, pre-loaded with the curated file. One went to a poet in Ullapool, a fierce chronicler of clearances. One went to a young, ferociously independent tech journalist in Glasgow who had been sued twice by government contractors. The last went to an elderly professor of moral philosophy in St Andrews, a man who believed in the language of right and wrong in a world that spoke only of efficiency.

The instructions were the same, typed on a sheet of plain paper: *If I am removed from office, or if I publicly denounce this document as a forgery, you are to decrypt this (passphrase: DryStoneDyke) and publish its contents in full, by any means necessary. You are now a keeper of a fire. Do not let it go out.*

He had created three separate, human tripwires. Three points of failure the Grayling system could not possibly have modelled, because it was built on networks, on data, on influence. It did not understand the stubborn, solitary conscience. It could not quantify a poet’s rage, a hacker’s pride, or an old man’s duty.

He returned to Edinburgh as dusk fell on the second day. The city felt like a set. He walked into Bute House, the target back in the crosshairs. Morag met his eyes, gave a tiny, almost imperceptible nod. The envelope was safe.

He had one day left.

The phone rang just after 10 PM. A private number. He knew who it was before he answered.

“First Minister.” The ghost’s voice was a thin wire, stretched to breaking. “They’ve moved. They know I spoke to you. They’re… sanitising.”
“Where are you?”
“It doesn’t matter. They’ll find me. I just… I wanted to hear a human voice. Before the interface takes over completely.” A shuddering breath. “Did you read it?”
“I read it.”
“And?”
“And I am sorry for the rock you were chained to.”
A silence. He thought he heard a sob, choked and dry. “Burn it, Alistair. Burn it all down.”
“I’m trying.”
“Try harder.” The line went dead.

He stood in the dark study, the dead phone in his hand. He looked out at the Edinburgh night. The castle was visible tonight, lit up, a picture-postcard fantasy of solidity and power. An illusion, like all the others.

He had the proof. He had distributed it. He had his tripwires. He had his stone.

Now he had to walk into the glass box tomorrow and sit across from the Prime Minister, with Cathcart’s neutral eyes upon him, and decide. To throw the stone was to unleash chaos, to guarantee his own destruction, to risk the fragile stability of everything. Not to throw it was to become part of the machine, to let its logic become his own, to accept the fiction as reality forever.

The ghost was gone, consumed by the system she helped build. He was alone with the detonator.

He poured a whisky. The ethanol, and the making, and the taking. He did not drink it. He set it on the desk beside the black USB drive.

Tomorrow, he would speak truth to power. And power, he now knew, would not listen. It would only calculate his words for risk, run them through its models, and generate a pathway to contain him.

He was not going to speak truth to power.

He was going to declare war on it.

And the first shot would be a whisper, not a bang. A single, simple phrase in a room of polished lies. He knew what it would be. He practiced it now, into the silent, listening dark.

“Prime Minister. Before we begin, I have a question about a piece of software. It’s called Grayling. Perhaps Sir Jeremy can explain how it decides the future of my country.”

He smiled, a cold, hard thing. Let the system model that.

The hook for the explosion was set. All that remained was to step back, and light the fuse.



---

# Chapter 22

## **CHAPTER 22**

The whisky he had not drunk the night before sat on the desk in his London hotel suite, a tawny accusation in a cut-crystal glass. The ice had long since melted, leaving the liquid pale and clouded. Alistair MacAulay ignored it. He was looking at his own reflection in the dark television screen, a ghost superimposed on the silent, empty news channel. He was rehearsing the silence.

The meeting was not in Downing Street. That was the first signal. It was in a secure conference room in the Cabinet Office, a place without history, without portraits, a bland, temperature-controlled box designed for the frictionless transaction of power. A glass box within a larger stone one. He had been here before, many times. He had always felt the subtle pressure of the air, the way it was engineered to leach passion from a man, to reduce conviction to a data point. Today, he intended to be an error in the code.

Sir Jeremy Cathcart met him in the antechamber. The Permanent Secretary looked, as ever, as if he had been assembled there minutes before from component parts of good wool and better tailoring. His neutrality was a weapon, polished to a blinding sheen.

“First Minister. I trust you slept well.”

“I trust you monitored the quality of my sleep, Jeremy. I’d hate for your metrics to have a gap.”

A blink. A micro-expression of recalibration. “The Prime Minister is waiting. He’s asked for this to be entirely off the record. No officials, no note-takers. Just the three of us.”

“A trinity,” MacAulay said, flatly. “How theological.”

The door opened without a sound.

Prime Minister Leo Thorne stood at the far end of a long, pale oak table, his back to them, looking out at a view of internal courtyards and ventilation shafts. He turned as they entered. He did not smile. He had the weary, focused energy of a surgeon about to operate on a contaminated limb. The charm, the practised bonhomie, had been switched off. This was the raw substrate.

“Alistair. Jeremy. Thank you for coming.” His voice was quiet, stripped of its public sonority. “Sit, please.”

They sat. MacAulay chose the chair opposite Thorne, leaving Cathcart to occupy the neutral space at the table’s flank, the observer, the arbiter. The room hummed, a low-frequency note from the hidden air systems.

Thorne steepled his fingers. He looked at MacAulay, a long, assessing look. “The devolution review papers are with the Cabinet Secretary. That’s not why I asked you here.”

“I didn’t think it was.”

“I read your latest briefing note. The one your office didn’t file through the proper channels. The one about… structural asymmetries in fiscal modelling.”

Cathcart shifted, a rustle of worsted. “Prime Minister, if I may—”

“You may not, Jeremy.” Thorne’s eyes never left MacAulay’s. “I want to hear from the First Minister. In this room. Man to man. What is it you want?”

MacAulay leaned back. The chair was too comfortable, designed to encourage passivity. He refused its embrace. “I want you to answer a question.”

“Ask it.”

“Grayling.”

The word hung in the processed air. It was not a question. It was a scalpel laid on the table between them.

Thorne’s expression did not change. He might have been told the time. He glanced at Cathcart. A flicker, a transfer of weight. “I’m not familiar.”

“Sir Jeremy is,” MacAulay said, turning his head slowly. “Aren’t you, Jeremy? The predictive governance platform. The algorithmic ghost in the machine. The one that models political outcomes, social stability indices. The one that has been running simulations on Scottish independence for a decade. The one that decided a slow, managed decline of certain powers was preferable to a negotiated settlement. The one that chained a brilliant woman to a rock until the vultures came.”

Cathcart’s face was a monument to composure, but a vein, thin and blue, had appeared at his temple. “First Minister, I think you’ve been misled by some rather fantastical source material. ‘Grayling’ is a codename for a discontinued research project into macro-economic forecasting. It was never operational.”

“A research project,” MacAulay repeated. He reached into his inner pocket and placed the black USB drive on the polished oak. It made a tiny, definitive click. “This contains every simulation Grayling ran on Scotland from its inception. It contains the decision trees, the weightings applied to variables like ‘cultural grievance’ and ‘resource nationalism’. It contains the override protocols, where human judgement was removed from the loop. It contains the logs of its communications with a system called Janus, which you use to model individual psychological profiles of key actors. My profile is in there. Yours is too, Prime Minister. It’s all in there.”

Thorne looked at the drive as if it were a dead insect. “Where did you get this?”

“From the ghost. Just before the system consumed her.” MacAulay’s voice was dry, devoid of triumph. This was not a revelation; it was an autopsy. “She built your god, Jeremy. And you made her worship it until it broke her. Then you decommissioned her. A human research project.”

Cathcart’s polish was cracking at the edges. “This is a gross breach of the Official Secrets Act. Theft of sensitive material. You are in possession of classified tools you cannot possibly understand.”

“I understand enough. I understand that for the last five years, every policy initiative affecting my country, from infrastructure spending to healthcare allocations to the wording of your speeches, Prime Minister, has been, in part, an output of this system. A system designed for one purpose: to preserve the Union by the most efficient means possible. Not the most just. Not the most democratic. The most *efficient*. And its core finding was that a certain level of managed discontent was optimal. That hope could be calibrated. That ambition could be priced.”

Thorne finally moved. He leaned forward, his hands flat on the table. “And what if it was? Do you think governments run on fairy dust and principle? We have a union to maintain. A global economy to navigate. Stability is not an abstract concept, Alistair. It is the ground upon which people build their lives. If a tool helps provide that stability, am I to reject it because it offends your romantic sensibilities?”

“It didn’t provide stability,” MacAulay said, his voice dropping to a near whisper. “It provided a simulation of it. While the foundations corroded. You weren’t governing. You were gardening. Pruning, shaping, directing growth according to a schematic drawn by a machine that thinks human longing is a variable to be balanced. You made us a problem set.”

“And you,” Thorne said, a cold fire now in his eyes, “what are you doing? You have this… this data. You could leak it. Cause a scandal. But you bring it here. To me. Why? What is it you want?”

MacAulay smiled then, the same cold, hard thing he had practiced in the dark. “I told you. I want you to answer a question. But the question isn’t about Grayling.” He paused, letting the hum of the room fill the space. “The question is: do you still believe you’re in control?”

Thorne stared. Cathcart was utterly still.

MacAulay continued. “You see, I’ve distributed the core of this material. To certain journalists, lawyers, academics. With instructions. It is in a digital dead man’s switch. If I do not reset that switch every twelve hours, it disseminates. The first batch is… contextual. The story of the ghost. The existence of Grayling. Enough to light a fire.”

“This is blackmail,” Cathcart hissed.

“It’s a tripwire,” MacAulay corrected. “I am here to give you a choice. The first choice is the obvious one. You deny, you threaten, you invoke the law. I walk out of here, and the story breaks. The scandal would be immense. The ‘secret computer ruling Scotland’? It would be the end of the devolution settlement. It would make independence an inevitability within months.”

“And your career,” Thorne said, “would be ashes. You’d be prosecuted.”

“Aye. I know the weight of the stone. I’m prepared to throw it, and be struck by the fragments. That is one path.”

“And the other?” Thorne’s voice was grit.

“The other is you publicly announce an immediate, full-scale, judge-led public inquiry into the use of algorithmic modelling in UK governance. Terms of reference set by the Scottish Parliament. Full disclosure. Full transparency. You dismantle Grayling, Janus, and every other silent partner in this room. You do it today.”

“Impossible,” Cathcart said. “The national security implications alone—”

“Are a fig leaf,” MacAulay snapped. “Your national security has been compromised by a cabal of technocrats playing God with a nation’s future. The greatest threat to the Union sits in this room, and it isn’t me. It’s the lie you built to preserve it.”

Thorne had gone pale. He was a calculator himself, and the numbers were not adding up. The variables were screaming. “You’re asking me to self-immolate.”

“I’m asking you to choose a real union over a simulated one. I’m asking you to govern, not manage. For the first time in a decade.” MacAulay stood up. He left the USB drive on the table. “That is your copy. Do with it what you will. I have mine.”

“Where are you going?” Thorne asked, the authority in his voice fraying.

“Back to Edinburgh. To wait. You have six hours before my next check-in is due. Then the first story goes live. The headline writes itself: ‘The Ghost in the Machine.’” He looked at Cathcart. “You should have listened to the rain, Jeremy. You can’t model the difference.”

He walked to the door. No one stopped him.

The silence he left behind was not the silence of the room’s engineering. It was a vacuum, a howling void where the certainties of a decade had just been sucked out into space.

In the antechamber, his protection officer fell into step beside him. “Everything alright, First Minister?”

“No,” MacAulay said. “But it’s truthful.”

As his car pulled out into the Whitehall traffic, the soft English drizzle began again. He watched it on the window. He did not think of stones. He thought of fuses, burning down in the dark.

***

In the glass box, the two men remained.

Leo Thorne picked up the USB drive. It was warm from MacAulay’s pocket. He turned it over in his fingers. “Is it real, Jeremy?”

Cathcart had poured himself a glass of water from the carafe on the table. His hand was steady, but the act was too deliberate, a performance of calm. “The data appears to be genuine. The breach is catastrophic.”

“He’s outmanoeuvred us.”

“He has gambled everything on a single, reckless play. He believes we are more afraid of the scandal than he is of prison.”

“Are we?”

Cathcart took a sip. “The system has contingencies, Prime Minister. Grayling may be compromised, but its successors are not. Janus is fully operational. We have a psychological profile on MacAulay that is twelve inches thick. We modelled this. Not this exact scenario, but the genus of it. The ‘Moral Crusader’ paradigm.”

Thorne looked at him with something close to disgust. “You modelled him. What does your model say he does next?”

“It says he is fundamentally consistent. He will not reset the dead man’s switch. He expects us to capitulate or to destroy him. He is prepared for either. His utility function is based on legacy, not survival.”

“So we lose. Either way, we lose.”

“Not necessarily.” Cathcart set down his glass. “The model also indicates a third-path probability, with a forty-two percent confidence interval. One it has labelled ‘Controlled Demolition’.”

“Explain.”

“We cannot prevent the story from breaking. But we can shape what the story *is*. We pre-empt him. We announce the inquiry ourselves, but on our terms. We frame Grayling as a rogue, experimental project, tragically misapplied by overzealous officials. We express profound regret. We sacrifice… manageable elements.”

“You.”

“If necessary. A knightfall is a recognised piece on the board. The inquiry, under our terms, takes years. It hears evidence in camera. It produces a report that is a masterpiece of obfuscation. By the time it concludes, the public’s appetite for the story will have been sated, the narrative controlled. MacAulay’s victory becomes a procedural quagmire. He gets his inquiry, but it becomes a tomb for the truth, not a platform for it.”

Thorne considered it. The political logic was impeccable. It was the logic that had kept him in power. It was the logic of the machine. “And him? What happens to MacAulay?”

Cathcart’s face was neutral again, the crack sealed. “In that scenario, his leverage is gone. He is a man who has achieved his stated goal. His moral capital is spent. The model predicts a high likelihood of political decline, or a move to a symbolic role. He becomes a monument, not a movement.”

“And if he refuses? If he releases the data anyway?”

“Then we execute the contingency we have for a Category-Five constitutional crisis. The Cabinet Office has prepared the legal instruments for the suspension of devolved government in the event of a systemic breakdown in relations. We would present it as a temporary measure, to allow for a cooling-off period and a reset. The public, faced with chaos versus order, will choose order. Especially if the alternative is presented as the chaos wrought by a fanatic with a stolen computer file.”

Thorne walked to the window. The courtyard was grey, rain-slicked. A controlled demolition. A suspension of parliament. These were not tools of government. They were tools of empire. He felt the weight of the drive in his palm. The making, and the taking.

“He asked if I believed I was still in control,” Thorne said, mostly to himself.

“You are, Prime Minister,” Cathcart said, his voice a soft, insistent pressure in the room. “You are choosing the form of the containment. That is the essence of control. MacAulay understands force. He does not understand… administration.”

Thorne turned. The decision settled on him, cold and heavy. It was not the weight of a stone. It was the weight of the whole damned dyke. “Do it. Draft the announcement for the inquiry. My terms. Not his. And prepare the suspension orders. Just in case.”

“Very good, Prime Minister.”

“And Jeremy?”

“Sir?”

“Find out how he got that data. Every link in the chain. I want them broken.”

Cathcart gave a small, precise nod. “The system is already tracing the provenance. It will find the leaks. And it will seal them.”

***

The flight to Edinburgh was a capsule of unnatural calm. MacAulay sat, staring at the clouds, a blanket of white wool below. He had lit the fuse. Now came the wait for the shockwave. He did not expect Thorne to capitulate. He expected a counter-move, something elegant and brutal from the deep state playbook. He was ready for it. Or so he told himself.

His phone, a secure device, buzzed just as they began their descent. A message from an encrypted account, one he recognised. It was from the lawyer, Anya, the one who held the dead man’s switch.

*> Proximity alert. Multiple, coordinated data-sweep attacks detected on the secure servers holding Package G. Sophisticated, state-grade. They are hunting the backups. They know about the switch.*

He typed back, his fingers cold. *> Can they disarm it?*

*> Not before the 18:00 deadline. But they are mapping the distribution network. They will find the recipients after the fact. They are preparing for the aftermath.*

Of course they were. They weren’t trying to stop the first explosion. They were planning to control the fire and arrest the arsonist.

*> Hold the line.*

*> Always.*

As the wheels touched the tarmac at Turnhouse, a different kind of message arrived. This one was on the public news wire. A headline flashed on his aide’s tablet, passed to him wordlessly.

**PM ANNOUNCES MAJOR PUBLIC INQUIRY INTO ‘GOVERNANCE IN THE DIGITAL AGE’**

He skimmed the bulletin. The language was masterful. A “forward-looking examination of the ethical frameworks required for modern statecraft”. A “commitment to transparency”. Regret for “any past over-reliance on experimental decision-support tools”. The inquiry would be led by a senior judge. Evidence would be heard from “all stakeholders”. It was a masterpiece of pre-emptive obfuscation. They had chosen Controlled Demolition.

His phone rang. A private number. He answered.

“First Minister.” It was Thorne’s voice, stripped of all pretence at warmth. “You saw the announcement.”

“I saw a magician’s handkerchief.”

“It’s your inquiry. You won. Call off your dead man’s switch. Let the process work.”

MacAulay watched the rain streak across the plane’s window. “My terms were for the Scottish Parliament to set the terms. This is your inquiry, on your terms. It’s a quarantine, not a cure.”

A long sigh, crackling down the line. “Alistair. This is the only way it happens. Take the win. Step back from the brink. Or I will have no choice but to take more direct measures to ensure stability.”

The threat was naked now. “Suspension.”

“I will not see the United Kingdom torn apart because of your ideological purity. Reset the switch. Stand down. This is your last chance to be a statesman, rather than a martyr.”

The line went dead.

MacAulay sat in the stationary plane, the engines whining down. They had shown him their move. A gilded cage for the truth. And a sword of Damocles over his parliament.

He had six hours.

He could stand down. The story of Grayling would become a footnote in a long, dull inquiry. He would be praised for his principled stand. He would become a monument.

Or he could let the switch fire. The story would erupt, a volcano of public outrage. Thorne would be forced to suspend Holyrood. He would be arrested. Scotland would be placed under direct rule. The chaos would be immediate, and profound.

The ghost’s voice echoed in his memory. *Burn it all down.*

But to burn it down was to burn the people in it. To stand down was to let the machine repair itself, stronger, more hidden.

There was a third way. The system could not model it, because it was not logical. It was human.

He turned to his aide. “Get me the Lord Advocate. Then, get me the Presiding Officer. I want a recall of Parliament for tonight. Emergency session.”

“On what grounds, First Minister?”

“On grounds of a fundamental democratic crisis. And tell the press I’ll be making a statement from my office in one hour.”

“What will you tell them?”

MacAulay looked out at the Scottish rain, lashing the tarmac. It was a hard, honest rain. It promised no easy outcomes.

“The truth,” he said. “All of it. Before they can build a tomb for it.”

He would not throw the stone into the glass house.

He would walk into the glass box of the parliamentary chamber, and he would shatter it from the inside with words. He would make the explosion public, live, and undeniable. He would force every member, every citizen, to look at the ghost in the machine. He would transfer the detonator from his hand to theirs.

It was not in the model. It was an act of faith.

As his car sped towards the city, towards the castle on the rock, he felt the weight lift. Not because the burden was gone, but because he had chosen how to carry it. He would not be a martyr or a monument.

He would be a witness.

And his testimony would begin with a single, simple phrase, broadcast to a nation. He practiced it now, under his breath, a whisper against the drumming rain.

“Today, I must tell you how a machine was built to think for us. And how we must now remember how to think for ourselves.”

The hook was set. The fuse was lit. And he was no longer holding it. He was the spark, flying into the powder keg.



---

# Chapter 23

## **CHAPTER 23**

The rain on the window of the First Minister’s office was Scottish rain. It came in horizontal, wind-driven volleys, rattling the triple-glazed pane, scouring the ancient basalt of the castle rock below. It was not an apologetic drizzle. It was an argument. Alistair MacAulay stood before it, his back to the room, watching the water’s relentless work. In an hour, he would walk into the glass box of the parliamentary chamber and speak the unspeakable. The fuse, he had thought, was lit. Now, he felt the cold length of it in his own veins.

The weight had not lifted. It had merely changed shape. From a stone to throw, it had become a mantle to wear, woven from every confidence, every secret, every fragile trust he was about to break on the floor of a televised democracy. To burn it down was to burn the people. To stand down was to let the machine repair itself. This third way—this act of public testimony—felt, in the grey, hammering light of the morning, like walking into a furnace with a paper shield.

“The Lord Advocate is on her way, First Minister.” Mhairi’s voice was stripped of its usual warmth, pared down to professional bone. “The Presiding Officer has agreed to the recall. The chamber will be full. The media… it’s a frenzy. They know it’s about the Algorithm. The leaks have seen to that.”

MacAulay did not turn. “And the UK Government?”

“Radio silence from Whitehall. Total. Sir Jeremy’s office says he is ‘in meetings for the remainder of the day.’ The Prime Minister’s press secretary has no comment.”

“Aye. They’re building the tomb. Brick by bureaucratic brick.” He saw their logic, clear and cold as the rain. Let the mad Scot rant. Let him produce his ghosts. Then, with solemn regret, pronounce him unstable, his evidence the paranoid fantasy of a man broken by the pressures of high office. Direct rule would follow, a medicalised coup.
