The system would not just repair; it would be reinforced, with MacAulay’s shattered credibility as the new foundation. The corruption was not in the act of theft, but in the architecture of plausible denial. It turned truth into a symptom.

“The draft statement.” Mhairi placed a single sheet of paper on the desk behind him. It was pitifully short. A confession of complicity, a sketch of the ghost, a surrender of power back to the Parliament. It ended with his resignation.

He had thought he would be a witness. Now he understood the final, corrosive twist of the lens: to speak truth to power, one must first admit one’s own power has been complicit in the lie. The corruption had not been an external infection. It was a symbiosis. He had fed the machine, and it had, in turn, shaped him. The comfortable path, the smoothed road, the poll-tested phrase—he had taken them all. The Algorithm had not forced him. It had merely made the cowardly choice look like the rational one.

“It’s not enough,” he said, still facing the storm.

“It’s what we agreed. The facts. The admission. The constitutional hand-grenade.”

“It’s a suicide note. And suicides are easily dismissed.” He turned. Mhairi looked exhausted, the skin taut around her eyes. She had staked her career on his integrity, and he was about to detonate the ground beneath them both. “Where is the proof, Mhairi? Not the theory. The thing you can hold in your hand. The stone.”

“We have the code. The Whistleblower’s files.”

“Data. Invisible. Deniable. They’ll say we fabricated it. That *I* fabricated it, to cover some deeper scandal.” He walked to the desk, but did not touch the statement. His fingers brushed the cold, smooth quartz of a paperweight, a gift from some forgotten conference. “Cathcart was right about one thing. Power isn’t about force. It’s about narrative. Who controls the story. Right now, their story is that I am a man on the edge, inventing conspiracies to explain my own failures. We need to give them a different story. One they cannot edit.”

“How?”

“We need a witness they cannot dismiss.”

The intercom on his desk buzzed, a sharp, electronic insect. “First Minister, the Lord Advocate is here.”

* * *

Catriona MacLeod, Lord Advocate for Scotland, was a woman carved from a different kind of stone. Where MacAulay was all weathered, volcanic crag, she was flint: sharp, precise, and capable of a spark. She did not sit. She stood before his desk, her black suit absorbing the room’s dim light, a slim briefcase held at her side like a weapon.

“Alistair. You have ignited a constitutional crisis. Possibly a riot. Explain to me why I should not, this instant, advise Her Majesty to prorogue this Parliament and have you removed for conduct unbecoming of your office.”

Her voice was low, legal, and carried the absolute chill of the bench. This was not the political ally, the occasional dinner guest. This was the chief legal officer of the Crown in Scotland, and her duty was to the law, not to him.

“Because the conduct unbecoming of this office,” MacAulay said, meeting her flinty gaze, “began long before I entered it. And because the riot, if it comes, will be because the people learn their sovereignty has been quietly, legally, auctioned off to a logic gate. I intend to tell them. Tonight. I am informing you, as the law requires, of a matter of grave public interest.”

“You are informing me you intend to breach the Official Secrets Act. To disclose classified intelligence protocols. To potentially collapse a cross-border security arrangement.” She listed the charges without inflection. “That is not public interest. That is sedition.”

“Is it sedition to expose a sedition against democracy itself?” He leaned forward, planting his hands on the desk. “Catriona, you’ve seen the summaries. You know what ‘The Caledonian Solution’ is. A machine that picks our winners, our policies, our future. A machine built in Whitehall, fed from Thames House, overseen by men like Jeremy Cathcart. It doesn’t just advise. It *decides*. And we, the elected government, have become its public relations department.”

She was silent for a long moment, her eyes scanning his face as if reading a difficult text. “Even if I believed your interpretation,” she said finally, “the evidence is… spectral. A disgraced academic’s files. Inferential chains. The testimony of a man who has every political motive to create a grand narrative of betrayal.”

“I know.” He nodded. “That’s why I need you.”

A flicker in her stony composure. “I am not your character witness.”

“No. You are the nation’s chief prosecutor. I am going to give you, live on television, a sworn testimony. And then I am going to give you evidence. Not inference. A transaction. A name. A moment where the ghost took flesh.”

He saw her mind working, the prosecutor’s instinct wrestling with the civil servant’s caution. “What name?”

“I will give it in the chamber. Under parliamentary privilege, yes. But also directly to you, in your capacity as Lord Advocate. I will allege a specific, criminal conspiracy to pervert the course of Scottish democracy. I will hand you the thread. And then I will dare you, in front of the entire country, to pull it.”

The room grew colder. The rain beat its tattoo. MacAulay watched her weigh the scales. Her career, the stability of the legal system, the Union itself, in one pan. In the other, the pure, abstract, terrifying principle of the law’s dominion. If the law was not sovereign over the secret state, then it was not sovereign at all. It was a theatre.

“You are asking me,” she said, each word measured, “to use my office to legitimise your… political explosion.”

“I am asking you to be present. To hear the allegation. To receive the evidence. What you do afterwards is a matter for you, and for the law. I am not asking for protection. I am asking for a witness with the authority to act. Without that, I am just a man shouting in a storm.”

She placed her briefcase on his desk, clicked the latches, but did not open it. “There is a precedent. The 1975 ‘Convention of the Crown’ opinion. It states that where a First Minister believes a fundamental tenet of the devolution settlement has been subverted by the Crown’s ministers in London, he may make a direct appeal to the sovereign people, prior to any legal finding, as a matter of democratic imperative.” She looked up. “It was never tested. It is a constitutional theory, not a statute.”

“It’s a stone,” MacAulay said softly. “And we are out of other missiles.”

Catriona MacLeod closed her eyes for a second. When she opened them, the spark was there. “I will be in the public gallery. I will listen. If you allege a crime, you will provide the evidence to my office, contemporaneously. I will then be obliged to investigate. That investigation will be independent. It may well include you.”

“Understood.”

“You realise,” she said, picking up her briefcase, “that this will be the end of you. Regardless of the truth. The machine does not tolerate a wrench in its gears. It will grind you to dust.”

MacAulay looked back to the window, to the honest, scouring rain. “Aye. But dust gets in the eyes. And in the circuitry.”

* * *

The drive to Holyrood was a passage through a city holding its breath. Police vans sat at discreet corners. The usual protestors outside the Parliament were swollen in number, their banners a confused jumble: “TRANSPARENCY NOW” tangled with “MACAULEY TRAITOR,” “ALGORITHM = TYRANNY” beside “NO 2ND REFERENDUM.” The narrative was already fracturing, the story slipping from anyone’s control. His driver took a back route, through the quiet, rain-slicked canyons of the Old Town.

The car phone rang. A private line. Mhairi, from the office.

“First Minister. We’ve just had a contact. From the Whistleblower.”

MacAulay’s grip tightened on the handset. “Go on.”

“He’s scared. The net is closing. He says Cathcart’s people are doing a ‘hard scrub’ of all linked systems. He’s going dark. But he sent one last thing. A packet. It arrived encrypted, via a dead-drop server. It’s… it’s a video file.”

“What’s on it?”

“He didn’t say. The subject line is ‘The Flesh Interface.’” Mhairi’s voice was thin with dread. “Do you want me to play it?”

MacAulay watched the high, grim walls of the Royal Mile slide past. The tomb was not being built in London. It was here, in these ancient stones, in the silence of a people betrayed. “No. Not yet. Send it to my secure device. I’ll view it before I go into the chamber.”

“First Minister… what if it’s not what we hope? What if it’s something worse?”

“There is no worse,” he said, and the truth of it settled in him, cold and final. “Only the full, unvarnished truth. Send it.”

* * *

His private office in Holyrood was a sanctuary of panic. The usual hum of government was gone, replaced by a strained, electric quiet. His senior staff moved like ghosts, avoiding his eye. The statement on his desk had been redrafted, expanded, legalised. It was now a formal indictment. It felt like his own obituary.

He locked the door. Took out the secure tablet. The file, labelled only with a string of numbers, sat in its inbox like a unexploded shell. He tapped it.

The video was dark, grainy, shot from a high angle—a security camera, or a hidden phone. The location was a bland, modern meeting room. A boardroom table. The time stamp in the corner was from eighteen months prior. During the last budget negotiations.

Three men sat at the table. Sir Jeremy Cathcart, his expression one of polite boredom. Opposite him, the then-Scottish Finance Secretary, a man named David Lyle, who had resigned six months later citing stress. And between them, a screen, on which was displayed a cascade of financial models.

Cathcart’s voice, tinny but clear: “—the modelling is unequivocal, David. The infrastructure spend north of Perth simply isn’t justifiable. It returns a net-negative on well-being metrics, and the political capital expenditure is high for marginal seat return.”

Lyle, rubbing his forehead: “The A9 is a death trap. The public demand is real. The committee—”

“The public,” Cathcart interrupted gently, “demand what the narrative tells them to demand. The narrative here is ‘efficiency versus sentiment.’ The Algorithm scores your proposed alternative—the rail upgrade in the Central Belt—twelve points higher on electoral impact. It’s not a choice. It’s an optimisation.”

“I have backbenchers. I have constituents.”

“And you have a career,” Cathcart said, his smile benign. “A career which, the modelling suggests, has a seventy-eight percent probability of cabinet promotion within two years if you champion the rail upgrade. If you insist on the A9… the probability drops to twenty-two. The system perceives it as parochialism. A lack of strategic vision.”

The video cut. Then another clip. A different room. Cathcart with a junior minister, discussing agricultural subsidies. The same pattern. The gentle, inexorable presentation of the Algorithm’s “optimal path,” coupled with a soft, unspoken gloss on the personal costs of deviation.

It was not a smoking gun. It was something subtler, more insidious. It was the corruption in its purest form: the replacement of argument with algorithm, of conviction with calculation, of political courage with personal risk assessment. It was the reduction of democracy to a management problem, and of ministers to middle-managers, terrified of a bad performance review from a machine they could not see.

The final clip was different. It showed Cathcart alone in what looked like a London club, leather armchairs, the glow of a fireplace. He was speaking to someone off-camera, his voice lower, confiding.

“The beauty of the system,” Cathcart said, sipping a brandy, “is that it absolves them of the burden of choice. They come to believe the optimal path is the *right* path. Morality becomes a subroutine of efficiency. Sovereignty is quietly transferred from the messy, emotional parliament to the clean, rational server farm. And they thank you for it. They sleep better at night.”

A voice off-camera, muffled, laughing: “And if they wake up?”

Cathcart’s face turned towards the hidden camera, his eyes catching the firelight, empty and serene. “Then we show them the numbers. We show them their own complicity. We remind them that they *chose* the smooth road. That they are not victims of a plot. They are beneficiaries of a system. And one does not bite the hand that feeds one a steady diet of power and re-election.”

The video ended.

MacAulay sat in the silence. The weight was back, a physical pressure on his chest. This was the true corruption. Not a conspiracy of villains, but a seduction of the capable. The ghost did not possess you. It persuaded you to sell your own soul for a quieter life, a surer win, a clearer path. It made cowardice look like wisdom, and surrender feel like strategy.

He had been seduced. He had looked at the polls, the models, the risk assessments. He had taken the smooth road.

The intercom buzzed. “First Minister. It’s time.”

* * *

The walk to the chamber was a march through a gauntlet of silent stares. MSPs clustered in the corridors, their conversations dying as he passed. The air was thick with a mixture of fear, curiosity, and a simmering anger. He saw allies who would not meet his eye. Opponents who watched him with a kind of grim satisfaction. The Presiding Officer, a small, stern woman in her robes, nodded to him once, her face unreadable.

He entered the chamber. The noise hit him first—a low, anxious rumble that fell into a dead, watchful hush as he took his place at the front bench. The glass walls of the public gallery above were packed, a mosaic of pale, intent faces. He saw Catriona MacLeod, sitting perfectly still, a dark column amidst the crowd. In the press gallery, a forest of lenses swivelled towards him, unblinking.

This was the glass box. And he was inside it.

The Presiding Officer called the emergency session to order. The formalities were a blur, a ritual incantation in a language that suddenly felt archaic, useless. Then she said, “I call the First Minister.”

MacAulay stood. He placed no notes on the lectern. He had memorised the bones of it. The flesh would have to be his own.

He looked around the chamber, at the faces of the people he had led, debated, fought, and compromised with. He saw the machine in every one of them—in their desire for order, for advantage, for safety. He saw it in himself.

“Presiding Officer,” he began, his voice quieter than he intended, forcing them to lean in. “Members of the Scottish Parliament. People of Scotland.”

He paused, letting the silence stretch, letting the weight of the occasion settle on them all.

“Today, I must tell you how a machine was built to think for us. And how we must now remember how to think for ourselves.”

He saw a few nods, faces hardening into prepared positions. They expected a rant against London. A blame-shifting exercise.

“But first,” he continued, “I must tell you how I let it think for me.”

The shift was seismic. A ripple of confusion went through the chamber. This was not in the script.

“For the past three years,” he said, his gaze travelling across the benches, “key decisions of this government—on budgets, on legislation, on strategy—have been guided, and in some cases determined, by a predictive algorithm known as ‘The Caledonian Solution.’ It was developed by UK intelligence. It was presented to me, and to my predecessors, as a tool. A sophisticated polling and modelling system to help us understand the consequences of our choices.”

He could feel Cathcart’s ghost in the room, whispering *They will thank you for it*.

“It was not a tool. It was an architect. It did not predict outcomes; it designed them. It did not analyse political reality; it sought to create a new one. A reality optimal for the stability of the Union, and for the electoral fortunes of those who followed its advice. It turned governance into a game with a pre-determined winner, and it convinced us that playing that game was leadership.”

A voice from the opposition benches, sharp: “And you went along with it?”

MacAulay turned to the speaker. “Yes. I did. We all did. When it told us to fund a hospital here and not a school there, we found justifications. When it steered us away from contentious constitutional reforms, we called it pragmatism. It fed us the path of least resistance, and we called it wisdom. Because it was easier. Because it worked. Because it made us feel, in a world of chaos, as if we were in control.” He looked down at his hands on the lectern. “The most profound corruption is not the one you are forced into. It is the one you choose for the sake of a quiet life. I chose it. This government, in a thousand silent, daily assents, chose it.”

The chamber was utterly silent now. The confession had disarmed them.

“But a machine,” he went on, his voice gaining strength, “is only as clean as its inputs. And this one was fed on data harvested without full consent, on biases its programmers called ‘stability metrics,’ on a definition of ‘the good’ that had more to do with equilibrium than justice. It created a ghost in our democratic machine. A ghost that whispered the ‘right’ answer into the ear of power. And power listened.”

He then did what he promised. He named names. Not the programmers, not the spies. He named the moment of interface. He described the meeting with David Lyle and the A9, citing the date, the room, the specific pressure applied. He alleged a systematic, covert influence campaign, designed to pervert the normal democratic decision-making processes of a devolved parliament. He called it, clearly and legally, a conspiracy.

“I have,” he said, looking up to the public gallery, to where Catriona MacLeod sat, “provided the Lord Advocate with evidence supporting this allegation. It is now a matter for the law.”

A storm of shouts erupted. Points of order. Cries of “Resign!” Demands for proof. The Presiding Officer’s gavel was a weak click against the tumult.

MacAulay waited for it to subside. When it did not, he simply raised his voice, not in a shout, but in the flat, carrying tone of a man stating a final fact.

“The proof is in the pattern! The proof is in the policies we passed against our own manifestos! The proof is in the strange consensus that always seemed to benefit the centre over the periphery, the safe seat over the marginal, the quiet voice over the angry one! The proof is in the hollowing out of genuine debate in this very chamber, because why debate when the optimal path is already known?”

He leaned into the microphone, his words dropping to a near whisper again, compelling them to listen.

“They will say I am mad. Or a traitor. Or both. They will say the evidence is fabricated. They will say this is a cynical ploy to destroy the Union. They will use every tool of the state to discredit me, and this Parliament, and the very idea that we can govern ourselves.”

He looked around, meeting as many eyes as he could, trying to imprint this moment on them.

“But they cannot discredit you. They cannot un-hear what you have heard today. The ghost is named. It is out in the open. And a ghost, once seen by daylight, loses its power. The machine only works in the dark.”

He took a deep breath. The final step.

“Therefore, I will not resign.”

A gasp, then a roar of outrage and surprise.

“I will not resign,” he repeated, louder, “because my resignation would be the next optimal move. It would allow the story to become about me—my flaws, my breakdown, my betrayal. It would allow the system to reset, with a new, chastened minister, and the same ghost in the same machine. No. My duty now is to stand here, in this box of glass and light, and be the flaw the system cannot smooth over. I am the error in the code. I am the human variable it cannot compute. And I will remain here, forcing this Parliament, and the people it serves, to look at that error, and to decide: do we want a government of humans, flawed and free? Or a government of ghosts, perfect and prisoners?”

He stepped back from the lectern. “I commend this statement to the chamber.”

The noise that exploded was unlike anything Holyrood had ever heard. It was not debate. It was a primal roar of shattered consensus. Members were on their feet, shouting, some at him, some at each other, some at the gallery. The Presiding Officer’s calls for order were lost. The glass walls seemed to vibrate.

MacAulay did not sit. He stood, hands by his sides, and absorbed the fury and the fear. He saw it in their faces—the dawning horror that the comfortable world of calculated risks and managed outcomes was gone. They were adrift in a sea of genuine, terrifying choice. The corruption of easy power was being stripped away, leaving the raw, painful burden of freedom.

In the public gallery, Catriona MacLeod rose. She did not look at the chaos below. She looked directly at MacAulay, gave a single, curt nod, and turned to leave. The law was now in motion.

As the security team moved to escort him from the chamber through a side door, for his own safety, MacAulay took one last look. This was the all-seems-lost moment. His career was ashes. His government was collapsing. The Union was cracking. Chaos was the only certainty.

But within the chaos, he saw it—the seed. In the eyes of a young backbencher, not shouting, but staring with a fierce, clear intensity. In the face of an older opponent, whose expression was not triumph, but a kind of grim, respectful shock. They had seen the ghost. And seeing it was the first step towards exorcism.

He had not given them a solution. He had given them a problem they could no longer ignore. He had transferred the detonator.

The spark had flown into the powder keg.

Now, it was up to the powder to decide whether to burn.

* * *

They took him to a secure room, a neutral space used for cross-party talks. It was soundproofed. The roar of the chamber became a distant, muffled sea. Mhairi was there, her face pale.

“It’s begun,” she said, handing him a tablet. News channels were split-screening: his face, frozen in mid-speech, and live shots of crowds gathering outside, of police lines, of London news anchors with grave, patronising tones. “The UK Prime Minister will address the nation in thirty minutes. The word ‘reckless’ is being used. ‘Constitutional vandalism.’ They’re pushing the madness narrative. Hard.”

MacAulay scrolled. The narrative was indeed solidifying, just as he predicted. But there were fractures. Some commentators were asking questions. Opposition MPs in Westminster were demanding statements. The word “Algorithm” was trending globally.

“And here?” he asked.

“The party…” She shook her head. “It’s a civil war. The cabinet is split. Some are calling for a vote of no confidence tomorrow. Others… others are standing by you. For now.”

He nodded. It was the first ripple. The machine’s smooth surface was breaking into unpredictable waves.

His private phone vibrated. A number he did not recognise. He answered.

“First Minister.” The voice was strained, breathless. The Whistleblower. “You did it. You actually did it.”

“Where are you?”

“Doesn’t matter. I’m gone. But listen. Cathcart isn’t waiting. The ‘hard scrub’ wasn’t just about data. It was about witnesses. They’re moving to the containment phase. They will try to discredit you with something… personal. Something undeniable. They’ve been building it. A narrative of personal collapse. Medical records. Edited footage. They’ll release it within hours.”

MacAulay felt a cold trickle down his spine. Of course. The final move. Not to debate the ghost, but to destroy the man who saw it. “What do I do?”

“You can’t fight it. It will be everywhere. All-consuming.” A pause, filled with static. “The only way out… is through. You made the truth public. Now you have to make *yourself* public. Every flaw. Every mistake. You have to be more human than they can possibly fake. You have to be… real. It’s the only thing the machine cannot replicate.”

The line went dead.

MacAulay put the phone down. He looked at Mhairi. “They’re going to come for me. Not with politics. With psychiatry. With scandal.”

She understood immediately. The colour drained from her face. “We can pre-empt it. Release your own medical records. Make a statement—”

“No.” He cut her off. “That’s playing their game on their board. Checking my flaws against their list.” He thought of the video, of Cathcart’s serene face in the firelight. *They are not victims of a plot. They are beneficiaries of a system.*

He was not a victim. He was a participant. And his testimony was not complete.

“Get me a live camera,” he said, his voice quiet but absolute. “Here. In this room. In thirty minutes, when the Prime Minister is addressing the nation to call me a madman, I will be on every other channel. Not as the First Minister. Not as a martyr. As a man. And I will finish the story.”

“What will you say?”

MacAulay walked to the window of the secure room. It looked out over the internal courtyard, a confined, grey space. The ultimate glass box.

“I will tell them,” he said, “about the weight of a stone.”



---

# Chapter 24

## **CHAPTER 24**

The camera was a small, black, unblinking eye. It sat on a tripod in the centre of the secure room, its lens a pit of darkness that drank the sterile, overhead light. Mhairi had procured it from the BBC’s Scottish Parliament unit, along with a sound engineer who had asked no questions, his face pale and set. He had set the levels, given a curt nod, and left. The feed was being routed not through any official channel, but through a mosaic of encrypted, volunteer-run servers Mhairi’s network had established. A ghost broadcast for a ghost story.

Alistair MacAulay stood before it, feeling the absurdity of the space. A bunker designed for state secrecy, now the stage for a confession. He wore no jacket. His shirt sleeves were rolled to the elbows. The scar on his forearm, a pale, ropey line from a childhood tussle with barbed wire, was visible. He had refused powder, refused a lectern, refused anything that might suggest an address. This was not that.

“Thirty seconds, First Minister,” Mhairi said from the shadows by the door. Her voice was a tight wire. She held a tablet, monitoring the other broadcast. The Prime Minister was in Downing Street’s pillared briefing room, surrounded by the severe, reassuring symbols of office. Waiting to speak to the nation.

MacAulay did not nod. He was looking at the camera, but he was not seeing it. He was seeing the rain on the car window, that first, soft London drizzle. He was feeling the weight of the stone. The choice had never been throw it, or don’t. The choice had been to recognise it was in your hand. To feel its truth. To stop pretending it was just part of the landscape.

“We’re live,” Mhairi whispered.

He took a breath that was not deep, but shallow, real. The kind of breath you take before you speak a difficult truth to a friend, not the measured inflation before a speech.

“My name is Alistair MacAulay,” he began, his voice rougher than he’d intended. No title. “And for the last hour, I have been in a secure facility, having revealed evidence of a conspiracy to subvert Scottish democracy. You will be told, very soon, that I am unwell. That I am broken by pressure, prone to fantasy, a danger to myself and to the public discourse. You will be shown documents, perhaps videos, that seem to prove this. They will be convincing. They are designed to be.”

He paused, not for effect, but because the words were stones he had to lift and place, one by one.

“I am here to tell you that I am, in fact, unwell. I am broken by pressure. I have made mistakes. I am flawed. But my illness is not a psychiatric condition. It is the sickness of complicity. My flaw is that I believed, for too long, that the stone in my hand was too heavy to throw.”

On the tablet screen, the Prime Minister began to speak. The sound was off, but the man’s face was a masterpiece of grave concern. MacAulay did not look at it.

“They will tell you I am hallucinating. Seeing ghosts in the machine.” He allowed the faintest, most tired smile to touch his lips. “They are correct. I have seen a ghost. It is the ghost of a man called Ewan MacLeod. A civil servant who asked a question he was not supposed to ask, and who was erased for it. Not by a villain in the shadows, but by a system operating exactly as it was designed to. A system that treats truth as a contaminant, and people as data points to be managed. I have seen the ghost of his question. And I have seen the living, breathing men who benefit from his silence. They are not monsters. They are administrators. They are knights of the realm. They are the polished, reasonable voice that tells you all rain is H₂O, so why make a fuss about the difference between a Scottish downpour and a London drizzle?”

He took a step closer to the lens. The image would be tight, intimate, unforgiving. Every line on his face, every fleck of grey in his stubble, would be a pixel in the picture.

“I have sat in rooms where the fate of millions was decided. And the overwhelming sensation was not of power, but of weight. A terrible, suffocating weight of procedure, of precedent, of risk assessment. The system is a machine for turning moral choices into technical problems. It is a drystane dyke, and we are the stones. Neatly fitted. Holding the line. And the greatest sin is not to be a weak stone, but to be a stone that questions the wall.”

He looked down at his hands, then held them up, turning them slowly before the camera. They were the hands of a man who had once worked stone, now soft, but marked.

“They have my medical records. They will tell you about the sleepless nights. The anxiety. The prescription for beta-blockers after the referendum result. All true. They have footage, probably edited, of me shouting in private meetings. Of me looking exhausted, despairing. All true. I have shouted. I have despaired. I have lain awake and felt the entire edifice of this union like a physical weight on my chest. If that is madness, then the definition of sanity is a quiet, comfortable lie.”

From the corner of his eye, he saw Mhairi’s hand go to her mouth. She was watching the tablet. The Prime Minister was holding up a document, gesturing with paternal disappointment.

“The Prime Minister is now showing you proof of my instability. He is a good man, doing his duty. Sir Jeremy Cathcart, who orchestrated the erasure of Ewan MacLeod and the corruption of your democracy, is also a good man, doing his duty. That is the heart of the corruption. It requires no mustache-twirling villainy. It requires only good men and women to believe that the integrity of the system—the wall—is more important than the integrity of the individual stone. That the ‘hard scrub’ of an inconvenient life is a regrettable but necessary bit of maintenance.”

He let the term hang in the silent room. *Hard scrub*. A clinical phrase for a murder.

“So here is my confession, my undeniable truth. I am afraid. I am tired. I have, in my life, been arrogant, short-tempered, and vindictive. I have put political calculation before personal loyalty. I have looked at a spreadsheet and seen a budget surplus, and not the people whose lives were narrowed to create it. I am guilty of being a politician. But I am not guilty of lying to you about this. The evidence I released is real. The conspiracy is real. And their response—to attack not the evidence, but the mind of the man who holds it—is the oldest, truest proof of its reality.”

He turned and walked to the window, showing the camera the bleak, grey courtyard. The glass box.

“They have placed me in here. A secure room. For my own protection, they will say. To contain the damage. It is the final, logical step. When the truth cannot be refuted, you must reframe the truth-teller. Diagnose him. Discredit him. Make his very being the subject of the debate, until the original crime is lost in the noise. It is a form of political psychiatry. And it works because we are complicit. We want our leaders to be sane, which we have conflated with being steady, predictable, *systemic*. A stone that fits.”

He turned back. His face was in shadow now, the light from the window behind him.

“Ewan MacLeod did not fit. So he was removed. I have stopped fitting. So I will be removed. That is the process. It is clean. It is efficient. It maintains the wall. But a wall built on silenced questions and erased lives is not a shelter. It is a tomb.”

He walked forward again, into the light, and sat on the edge of the table they used for briefings. An informal, almost vulnerable pose.

“You have a choice now. You can believe the Prime Minister. You can believe the documents, the experts, the serene, reasonable narrative of a man who has sadly, tragically, lost his grip. It is the easier path. It returns you to a familiar world. Or you can believe a tired, flawed, afraid man telling you a difficult, ugly story. You can believe that the ghost is real. And if you believe that, then you must ask yourself: what other ghosts are in the machine? What other questions have been ‘hard scrubbed’? What does your own silence weigh?”

He fell silent. The only sound was the faint hum of the air conditioning. The camera’s light glowed steady red.

“I do not know what happens next. To me. To this. But I know this: the weight of the stone is only yours if you choose to hold it. You can put it down. You can walk away from the wall. Or you can finally, finally, throw it.”

He held the camera’s gaze for a three-count of that shallow, human breath.

“I threw mine today. It is done.”

He gave a single, slow nod. The light on the camera went out.

For a long moment, the room was perfectly still. Mhairi lowered the tablet. The Prime Minister was still speaking, but his audience was now bifurcated, a nation split between two realities.

“They cut him off,” she said, her voice hollow. “Mid-sentence. The broadcast switched back to the studio. The anchors look… shell-shocked.”

MacAulay felt nothing. Not relief, not fear. A vast, echoing emptiness. He had thrown the stone. The arc of it was now beyond him.

The door to the secure room hissed open. Not Mhairi’s doing. Two men in the dark suits of the Protection Command stood there. Their faces were professionally blank, but their eyes were not. They had watched the broadcast.

“First Minister,” the senior one said. “We need to move you.”

“Am I under arrest?”

“No, sir. Protective custody. For your own safety. The situation… is volatile.”

MacAulay looked at Mhairi. Her eyes were bright with unshed tears, but her chin was up. She gave him the slightest nod. *Through.*

He stood, his joints stiff. He did not put on his jacket. He walked towards the door, the two officers falling in beside him. As he passed into the corridor, he saw Sir Jeremy Cathcart.

The Permanent Secretary was standing a little way down the hall, as if he had just arrived. He was not looking at MacAulay, but at his phone, his thumb scrolling calmly. He wore the same well-cut overcoat of authority. The system, personified. Unruffled. He did not glance up as the discredited First Minister was led past.

They took him not to a cell, but to a safe house. A modest, anonymous flat in a Edinburgh New Town building. The windows were reinforced. The doors were solid. It was another, softer glass box. His guards were polite, distant. They called him “sir” and made him tea. One of them, a young man with a Fife accent, silently placed a copy of that day’s *Scotsman* on the kitchen table. The headline was a single word: **GHOST**.

MacAulay sat at the table, the tea going cold. He did not turn on the television. He did not need to. The silence of the flat was its own broadcast. He thought of Ewan MacLeod, not as a ghost, but as a man who had probably sat in a room not unlike this one, feeling the walls of the system close in. He had been erased. MacAulay had been amplified, then diagnosed. The methods had evolved. The purpose was constant.

Night fell. The city lights glittered beyond the reinforced glass. His personal phone, which they had not taken, began to vibrate. Not with calls, but with a torrent of notifications. Emails, messages, social media tags. A world reacting in real-time. He did not look at it.

There was a knock at the internal door. The senior protection officer entered. “Sir, there’s… you have a visitor. It’s… unusual.”

“Who?”

“The Lord Advocate, sir.”

MacAulay frowned. The head of Scotland’s independent prosecution service. A woman of formidable intellect and glacial impartiality, Morag Kincaid. She was not part of his government. She was part of the state.

“Show her in.”

Morag Kincaid entered the safe house as if it were her own chambers. She was a small woman in her sixties, with a helmet of steel-grey hair and eyes the colour of a winter sea. She carried a slim briefcase. She dismissed the protection officer with a look.

“Alistair,” she said. Not *First Minister*.

“Morag. To what do I owe the pleasure?”

“I watched your broadcast,” she said, placing the briefcase on the table. “And I have spent the last six hours with a team of prosecutors and police officers, reviewing the material you released. The raw data. Not the summary.”

“And?”

“And I have come, in a personal capacity, to tell you something.” She sat down opposite him, folding her hands. “The evidence is compelling. It is forensically sound. The chain of custody for the financial data is unbroken. The testimony from the anonymous source—this ‘M’—corroborates elements we have suspected but could never pin down. Ewan MacLeod’s disappearance is now, in my professional opinion, a homicide inquiry. The corruption of the devolution settlement is a live conspiracy case.”

MacAulay felt the air leave his lungs. It was not vindication. It was something heavier. Responsibility.

“The Prime Minister—”

“Is the Prime Minister of the United Kingdom,” she interrupted, her voice precise. “He has no authority over the Lord Advocate’s prosecution service in Scotland. He can call you mad. Westminster can vote no confidence. But he cannot stop me from issuing warrants, if the evidence merits it.”

“Does it?”

She looked at him for a long, piercing moment. “You asked people to choose which story to believe. I deal in evidence. The evidence points to crimes. Serious crimes. Perverting the course of justice. Misconduct in public office. Conspiracy. The subjects of the investigation would be senior civil servants. Possibly politicians. The political consequences would be… profound.”

“The union,” MacAulay said quietly.

“Would be under a strain for which there is no precedent,” she confirmed. “This is not a devolution review. This is a fracture. You understand that? You have not simply thrown a stone at a wall. You have placed a charge at its foundation.”

He thought of Cathcart’s serene face in the firelight. *Beneficiaries of a system.* “The system will protect itself.”

“It will try,” Morag Kincaid said. She opened her briefcase and took out a single sheet of paper. It was a warrant. The name at the top was *Sir Jeremy Cathcart*. “It will try with every legal, political, and media tool at its disposal. It will paint this as a nationalist witch hunt. It will question my impartiality. It will tie us in legal knots for years. The process will be the punishment—for me, for the service, for the very idea of Scottish judicial independence.”

She slid the warrant across the table.

“I am required to inform the relevant authorities before executing this. In this case, that includes the Permanent Secretary’s own department in London. The moment I do, the machinery will engage. He will be ‘too ill’ to be interviewed. Documents will be lost. Witnesses will become unavailable. The wall will close ranks.”

MacAulay looked at the warrant. It was just paper. But it was a stone of a different kind.

“Why show me?”

“Because you started this,” she said, not unkindly. “You made it public. You made it *political* in the deepest sense. The legal path is now inextricable from the political one. I can issue this warrant. But for it to mean anything—for it to not be immediately smothered by the system it seeks to indict—it needs a political reality to sustain it. It needs a parliament. It needs a government. It needs a people who have chosen to believe the ghost.”

She stood up. “I will leave this with you. I am required to give twelve hours’ notice before execution. I will make that notification at 9 a.m. tomorrow. You have until then.”

“To do what?”

“To decide if the wall you have cracked is ready to fall.” She picked up her briefcase. “You spoke of the weight of the stone, Alistair. This,” she gestured to the warrant, “is the weight of the consequence. It is heavier. Goodnight.”

She left. The flat was silent again. The warrant lay on the table like a landmine.

MacAulay walked to the window. The city was quiet. The ancient stones of Edinburgh were hidden by darkness and modern glass. A kingdom of ghosts. He thought of the very first stone, the one in his mind’s hand in the London car. The choice had never been simpler than he’d pretended. It had always been this. Not one throw, but an avalanche.

His phone buzzed again, insistently. This time, he looked. One notification rose above the torrent. A text from an unknown number.

***They have M. The flat in Leith. Come alone. Or the hard scrub completes. – A friend.***

The cold trickle down his spine became a flood. Mhairi. They hadn’t gone for him. They’d gone for the source. The witness. The containment phase was not about discrediting him anymore. It was about erasing the evidence. Permanently.

He looked at the warrant. Then at the door. The two protection officers were outside, their job to keep him safe inside the glass box.

The weight was no longer in his hand. It was in his chest. It was the weight of a friend.

He picked up the warrant, folded it, and put it in his pocket. Then he walked to the door, opened it, and looked at the senior officer.

“I need to leave.”

“Sir, our orders are—”

“Your orders are to protect me. The greatest threat to me right now is in this room. It is inaction. I am leaving. You can try to stop me, or you can help me. But you cannot contain this anymore.”

The officer stared at him. He had watched the broadcast. He had seen the Lord Advocate arrive. He was a Scottish police officer.

“Where do you need to go, sir?”

“Leith.”

The officer exchanged a look with his colleague. The younger one from Fife gave a barely perceptible nod.

“We’ll drive you, sir. But not in the official car. And we never had this conversation.”

The rain on the window of the unmarked car *was* Scottish rain. It was a hard, relentless, honest downpour, battering the cobbles of the New Town, streaming down the gutters towards the Forth. MacAulay watched it cleanse the world beyond the glass. He was thinking about the weight of a warrant in his pocket, and the weight of a life in his hands. A weight that promised no simple outcome, only the certainty of consequence.

The car moved through the sleeping city, a ghost itself. The journey was not a return to the beginning. It was a spiral, descending from the high, abstract walls of state to the wet, black asphalt of a side street in Leith. The power corruption that began in polished rooms in Whitehall ended here, in the threat against one woman in a flat.

The car stopped a street away. “We’ll wait here, sir. Five minutes. If you’re not back, we’re coming in.”

MacAulay got out. The rain soaked him immediately. It felt like absolution. He walked to the address, a sandstone tenement door. It was slightly ajar.

He pushed it open and stepped into the close. The light was broken. The only illumination came from the open door of a ground-floor flat at the rear. Mhairi’s safe house.

He walked towards it, his footsteps echoing on the stone flags. The doorframe was splintered. Forced.

He stepped inside.

The flat was a wreck. Books pulled from shelves, cushions slashed, the computer smashed. And in the centre of the room, sitting calmly on the only upright chair, was Sir Jeremy Cathcart.

He was not polished now. His hair was slightly dishevelled. There was a smudge of dust on his coat. In his hand, he held a small, old-fashioned, but very effective-looking pistol. It was pointed at Mhairi, who sat on the floor, her back against the wall, a trickle of blood from her lip. Her eyes were wide with fear, but also with a furious, defiant intelligence.

“First Minister,” Cathcart said, his voice still the epitome of reasonable calm, though edged with a faint, metallic tension. “I had a feeling you’d come. The personal loyalty flaw. I did note it in your profile.”

“Where are your people, Jeremy?”

“Oh, they’re nearby. Containing the area. This is a private chat. The last one, I think.” He gestured with the gun. “Please, close the door.”

MacAulay did. The room became a sealed box. Just the three of them.

“You’ve been busy, Alistair. The live broadcast was a… theatrical masterstroke. Turning your flaws into a virtue. Making pathology into credibility. I admire the technique. But it changes nothing. The system is already adjusting. New narratives are being woven. The Lord Advocate’s little foray will be framed as a tragic, politically motivated overreach. She will be offered a dignified retirement. The warrants will vanish.”

“Like Ewan MacLeod.”

“Like Ewan MacLeod,” Cathcart agreed. “A necessary subtraction. As is this.” He glanced at Mhairi. “Your source. The last loose thread. Once pulled, the entire tapestry you’ve tried to weave unravels. It becomes the sad, fictional obsession of a broken man.”

MacAulay felt the warrant in his pocket. Paper against a gun. “It’s too late, Jeremy. The evidence is out. People have seen it.”

“People see what they are told to see. Tomorrow, they will be told that the evidence was fabricated. By her.” He nodded at Mhairi. “A radical activist with a grudge, exploiting a vulnerable First Minister. We have her history. The emails we’ve planted on her server are quite convincing. She manipulated you, Alistair. She fed your paranoia. It’s a much cleaner story. It preserves the wall. And it allows you a way out. A quiet resignation on health grounds. A dignified retreat. You can even keep your pension.”

The offer hung in the dust-filled air. The final deal. The system’s mercy. A return to silence.

MacAulay looked at Mhairi. She gave a tiny shake of her head. *Don’t.*

He looked back at Cathcart. The man was the system. Not its master, but its most perfect expression. He believed in the wall. He would kill for it, not out of passion, but out of principle.

“You told me once,” MacAulay said, his voice quiet in the ruined room, “that they were not victims of a plot, but beneficiaries of a system. You were right. You are the beneficiary. And she,” he pointed to Mhairi, “is the victim. And I have been the participant. But you made a mistake, Jeremy.”

“Oh?”

“You made it personal. You came here. You got your hands dirty. You stepped out of the polished room and into the rain. You’re not an administrator now. You’re just a man with a gun in a wrecked flat.”

Cathcart’s smile was thin. “Semantics. The outcome is the same.”

“Is it?” MacAulay took the warrant from his pocket. He didn’t unfold it. He just held it up. “The Lord Advocate is issuing this for your arrest at 9 a.m. Conspiracy. Misconduct. Murder. She knows I’m here. If I don’t walk out, neither do you. The avalanche starts tonight.”

For the first time, a crack appeared in Cathcart’s neutrality. A flicker in his eyes. He had not anticipated this. The law, moving independently. The system had subsystems. And they could conflict.

“Then it seems,” Cathcart said slowly, “we are at an impasse.”

“No,” said a voice from the doorway.

The door had opened silently. Morag Kincaid stood there, flanked by four armed police officers from the Specialist Crime Division. Their weapons were trained on Cathcart.

“The impasse is resolved by due process, Sir Jeremy,” she said, stepping into the room. She looked at the gun, at Mhairi, at the wreckage. Her winter-sea eyes were frozen. “You are under arrest. Put the weapon down.”

Cathcart looked from MacAulay to the Lord Advocate to the officers. He saw the architecture of his own system turning against him. The wall, cracking from within. The benefit of the system was conditional on remaining within its rules. He had broken them. He had become a loose thread.

He did not put the gun down. He turned it, very deliberately, away from Mhairi. Not towards the officers, but towards his own temple.

“Jeremy, don’t—” MacAulay took a step forward.

“It’s cleaner,” Cathcart said, his voice regaining its eerie calm. “A final bit of maintenance. The hard scrub. No trial. No spectacle. Just a sad end for a loyal servant who couldn’t bear the strain. The wall remains.”

His finger tightened on the trigger.

“The wall is a lie!” MacAulay shouted, the words ripped from him. “It always was! It’s just stones! And every stone has a name! Ewan MacLeod! Mhairi Sinclair! You! Me! There is no wall without us!”

Cathcart hesitated. For a fraction of a second, the absolute certainty in his eyes wavered. He was a man who dealt in abstracts—systems, stability, the greater good. The shouting of names was a chaos he could not compute.

In that fraction, the young officer from Fife, who had entered behind the Lord Advocate, moved. Not with his gun, but with a tackle, low and fast. He crashed into Cathcart, and the pistol discharged into the ceiling with a deafening roar.

Then it was over. Cathcart was on the ground, pinned, the weapon kicked away. He did not struggle. He lay there, breathing heavily, his face pressed against the dusty floorboards of the flat he had come to clean.

Morag Kincaid walked over and knelt beside him. “Sir Jeremy Cathcart, I am arresting you on suspicion of conspiracy to pervert the course of justice, misconduct in public office, and the attempted murder of Mhairi Sinclair. You do not have to say anything…”

The words continued, the ritual of the law. MacAulay went to Mhairi, helping her up. She was shaking, but her eyes were on Cathcart, watching the system read its own rights to one of its chief architects.

Outside, the Scottish rain continued to fall, washing the street clean.

***

The dawn broke over a different country.

MacAulay stood at the window of his Bute House study, back in the official residence. He was still First Minister, for now. The political machinery was in spasm. Westminster had recalled Parliament. The Scottish Parliament was assembling in emergency session. The media was a roaring fire of conflicting truths.

But in the quiet of the study, there was only the grey light and the aftermath.

The warrant had been executed. Cathcart was in a cell, being questioned. Mhairi was safe, giving her statement. The evidence was no longer a ghost story; it was a case file, moving with the grinding, inexorable speed of the law.

The door opened. Mhairi entered, a plaster on her lip, a fresh notebook in her hand. “The press office is drowning. The SNP group is meeting in an hour. The Unionist parties are calling for your immediate resignation and a Westminster takeover. The Lord Advocate has made a preliminary statement. It’s… seismic.”

“Is it true?” he asked, not turning from the window.

“Which part?”

“That the wall is ready to fall.”

She came to stand beside him. They looked out at the Edinburgh garden, the first buds of spring defiant in the chill air. “The foundation is cracked. The legal process has its own momentum now. But the political wall… that’s up to them.” She nodded towards the window, towards the city, the country beyond. “They heard you. They heard Cathcart. They have to choose what to believe. Not just about the past, but about the future.”

He thought of the stone. It was no longer in his hand. It was in the hands of millions. A collective weight. They could let it drop. Or they could use it to build something new, something that did not require ghosts in its foundations.

“What do I do now?” he asked, the question not political, but profoundly personal.

“You finish the story,” she said. “You go to Parliament. You stand before them, not as a martyr or a madman, but as the witness. You tell them about the weight. And then you let go.”

He nodded. The journey had begun in a London car, with the feeling of exile. It ended here, at home, with the feeling of a burden shared. Not a return, but a spiral into a deeper, more difficult truth. The corruption of power was not that it made men evil. It was that it convinced good men that the wall was more important than the stones.

He turned from the window. “Get me my jacket.”

“Which one?”

“The one I wore in London. I want them to remember where this started.”

As he walked out of the study, towards the waiting car that would take him to the chamber where his political life would end and something else might begin, he felt the ghost of Ewan MacLeod in the corridor. Not as a phantom, but as a presence. A question that had finally, after all the silencing years, received an answer.

The rain had stopped. The sky was the colour of a fresh-cut stone.
